Preponderance Requires a Cause
Razi refutes the philosophical objection about preponderance.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Razi argues that preponderance of one action over another always requires a preponderator, and how this principle supports the existence of a Creator.

Core Idea
Preponderance without a cause would undo the need for a Creator.

This passage tackles a fundamental question: when a free agent chooses one action over another, what determines the choice? The philosophers object that perhaps no cause is needed. Razi responds with two powerful arguments—one metaphysical and one empirical—showing that preponderance without a preponderator is impossible, and that this would undermine the very concept of a Creator.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that Razi presents the philosophers' objection first, then refutes it.
2
Identify the two proofs
The passage contains two distinct proofs against the claim that preponderance can occur without a preponderator. Find each one and see how they differ: one is metaphysical, the other empirical.
3
Reflect on the implications
Ask yourself: why does Razi consider this argument so important? How does it connect to the existence of a Creator? Think about the principle of causality.
This argument is foundational for the kalam proof of the existence of God.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وههنا للفلاسفة سؤالات السؤال الأول: أن هذا القادر المحكوم عليه بأنه يصح منه الفعل بدلا عن الترك، ويصح منه الترك بدلا عن الفعل، إما أن يكون رجحان أحد طرفي الفعل والترك على الطرف الآخر موقوفا على انضمام مرجح إليه أو لا يكون كذلك. لا جائز أن يقال : إنه لا يتوقف ذلك الرجحان على المرجح ويدل عليه وجهان: الأول : أنه لو حصل رجحان أحدى الطرفين على الآخر من غير مرجح، قد حصل الممكن من غير مرجح ، وذلك يفضي على نفي الصانع. والثاني: أنا لما وجدنا أنه ما لم يحصل في القلب ميل إلى أحد الطرفين لم ذلك الطرف على الآخر، ومتى صار الميل إلى الحركة إلى هذا الجانب وإلى الحركة إلى الجانب الآخر على التساوي، لم يترجح أحدهما على الآخر، بل بقي الإنسان في موضعه الذي هو فيه ساكنا متحيرا إلى أن يظهر المرجح، فحينئذ يحصل الرجحان، فظهر بما ذكرنا أن القول بأنه يجوز حصول رجحان أحد الطرفين على الآخر من غير مرجح باطل وفاسد.
And here there are questions from the philosophers. The first question: That this powerful (al-qadir) who is described as being capable of action instead of refraining and capable of refraining instead of action – either the preponderance (rujhan) of one of the two sides of action and refraining over the other is dependent on the addition of a preponderator (murajjih) to it or it is not so. It is not permissible to say that this preponderance does not depend on the preponderator, and two proofs indicate that: First, that if preponderance of one of the two sides over the other occurs without a preponderator, then the possible (al-mumkin) would occur without a preponderator, and that leads to the negation of the Creator (nafy al-sani'). Second, that we observe that as long as no inclination (mayl) to one of the two sides occurs in the heart, that side does not [preponderate?] over the other, and when the inclination to movement to this side and to movement to the other side become equal, neither preponderates over the other, but the human remains in his place, stationary, bewildered, until the preponderator appears, then preponderance occurs. So it is clear from what we have mentioned that the statement that it is permissible for preponderance of one side over the other to occur without a preponderator is false and corrupt.
Source pages p. 123

Commentary
This passage presents a philosophical objection and Razi's refutation. The objection is about the concept of preponderance (rujhan) in a free agent. The philosophers ask: when a qadir can either act or refrain, is the preponderance of one option over the other dependent on a preponderator (murajjih) or not? Razi argues that it must depend on a preponderator, and he gives two proofs. The first is a metaphysical argument: if preponderance could occur without a cause, then the possible (possible occurrences) would happen without a preponderator, which negates the need for a Creator (since all contingent things require a cause). The second is an empirical argument: humans experience that without an inclination (mayl) in the heart, no action is chosen; when inclinations are equal, one remains paralyzed until a preponderator tips the balance. Thus, the claim that preponderance can happen without a cause is false. This argument is crucial for establishing that all contingent occurrences require a cause, and for understanding the nature of free choice.

Key Terms
راجح
مرجح
رجحان
ممكن
صانع
ميل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Free will means that actions can occur without any cause at all.
Correction: Razi shows that even in free choice, preponderance (the actual selection) requires a preponderator, which is the motive or inclination. The cause is internal, not absent.

Checkpoint
According to Razi, what is the consequence of allowing preponderance of one act over its alternative without a preponderator?
A) It proves the existence of the Creator.
B) It leads to the negation of the Creator.
C) It affirms free will.
D) It validates the philosophical position.

Key Takeaway
Razi establishes that preponderance always requires a preponderator, thereby grounding causality in the free agent's choice and linking it to the necessity of a Creator.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of a major decision you made that changed your life. What was the decisive factor that tipped you toward that choice? Was that factor a preponderator? Now ask yourself: did you ever attribute that decision to mere chance or luck, ignoring the cause? How does Razi's argument challenge you to see God's role in the very structure of your choices?
?
You often say 'I don't know why I did that' or 'It just happened.' But this lesson says preponderance without a cause is impossible. Where in your life are you avoiding responsibility by pretending your actions have no cause? What would it mean to honestly trace your choices back to their real preponderators—even the ones you are ashamed of?
?
If preponderance requires a cause, then your faith in God is either a preponderated choice or a groundless whim. Which is it? Have you ever examined the reasons (preponderators) that tip your heart toward belief or away from it? Stop now and list one real preponderator that sustains your faith today. If you cannot name one, what does that imply?

Action Becomes Necessary When All Preponderants Are Present
Razi presents the philosophers' argument that action becomes necessary when all preponderants are present.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical argument that if a free agent possesses all preponderants (perfect power, decisive will, etc.), the action becomes necessary, and the implications for the distinction between a free agent and a necessitating cause, especially regarding God.

Core Idea
When all preponderants are present, the act must occur necessarily, erasing the difference between agent and necessary cause.

This passage presents a strong philosophical objection against the Ash'ari notion of a free-acting agent. It argues that if God has all the preponderants (perfect power, decisive will, etc.), His action becomes necessary, leaving no room for genuine freedom. Understanding this argument is crucial for appreciating subsequent theological responses.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing, then read the English translation. Let the logical flow come through first.
The passage is a continuous argument; note its structure.
2
Identify the logical steps
List the main steps: definition of complete preponderants, the conditional about refraining, infinite regress argument, conclusion of necessity, and the comparison between qadir and mujib.
Pay attention to the 'if then' structure.
3
Reflect on the implication for God
Consider the final paragraph: how the argument applies to God differently because His essence does not depend on external conditions.
This connects to later refutations.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما القسم الثاني : وهو أنه لا بد في هذا الرجحان من مرجح فنقول : إذا حصلت المرجحات بأسرها - وهي القدرة التامة و الإرادة الجازمة الخالية عن الفتور، والوقت والآنة ،والمصلحة وإزالة الموانع العقلية والشرعية، فمع حصول هذه المرجحات إما يكون الترك ممكنا أو غير ممكن فإن كان الترك ممكنا فمع حصول هذه المرجحات تارة يحصل الفعل وتارة يحصل الترك، فاختصاص أحد الوقتين بالفعل والآخر بالترك إما أن يتوقف على مرجح لأجله اختص أحد الوقتين بالفعل، والآخر بالترك أو لا يتوقف فإن توقف على مرجح لم يكن الحاصل الأول مرجحًا تاما، وكنا قد فرضناه كذلك، هذا خلف.

وأيضا : فلنفرض حصول هذا المرجح ، فحينئذ إما أن يكون حصول هذا الفعل هذا الوقت جائزاً أو واجباً، فإن كان جائزا عاد التقسيم الأول، وافتقر إلى مرجح آخر، ولزم التسلسل وهو محال، ولما بطل هذا ثبت أن الفعل واجب الحصول عند حصول كل المرجحات وممتنع الحصول عند اختلال قيد من القيود المعتبرة في الترجيح

وعلى هذا التقدير فالقادر حال ما حصلت المؤثرات بأسرها عقلا أن يجب يصدر عنه الأثر ويمتنع أن لا يصدر، وحال ما لم توجد المؤثرات بأسرها يجب عقلا أن لا يصدر عنه الأثر، ويمتنع الأثر، ويمتنع أن يصدر، وعلى هذا التقدير لا يبقى فرق البتة بين القادر والموجب بل الفرق أن شرائط التأثير في حق القادر سريعة التغير، وإذا حصلت بعد أن كانت معدومة صار القادر واجب التأثير، وإذا زالت بعد أن كانت موجودة صار ممتنع التأثير، إلا أن هذا التغير إنما يعقل في حق من تكون مؤثريته موقوفة على شرائط منفصلة عن ذاته، والباري تعالى قبل تأثيره في غير ليس موقوفا على شرائط منفصلة عن ذاته ؛ لأنه تعالى مبدأ لكل ما سواه، فلا يكون تأثيره فيما سواه موقوفا على شيء منفصل عنه، فلا جرم كان تأثيره في غيره يخص ذاته، وذاته ممتنعة التغير فكان تأثيره في غيره أيضًا ممتنع التغير.

وهذا هو السؤال القوي الذي عليه يعولون وبه يصولون.
As for the second division: namely that this preponderance requires a preponderant. We say: when all the preponderants are obtained—namely perfect power, decisive will free from weakness, time and the instrument, benefit, and removal of intellectual and legal obstacles—then with the obtaining of these preponderants, either refraining is possible or not possible. If refraining is possible, then with these preponderants sometimes the act occurs and sometimes refraining occurs. So the specification of one time for the act and another for refraining either depends on a preponderant due to which one time is specified for the act and the other for refraining, or does not depend. If it depends on a preponderant, then the first obtained [preponderant] was not a complete preponderant, but we had assumed it to be so. This is a contradiction.

Also: let us assume obtaining this preponderant. Then either the occurrence of this act at this time is possible or necessary. If it is possible, the first division returns, and it needs another preponderant, leading to infinite regress, which is absurd. Since this is false, it is established that the act is necessarily obtained when all preponderants are obtained, and impossible to obtain when any condition considered in preponderance is missing.

On this basis, the powerful one (al-qādir) when all influences are obtained is rationally such that the effect must issue from him and it is impossible that it not issue; and when not all influences are obtained, it is rationally necessary that the effect not issue from him, and impossible that it issue. On this basis, there remains no difference at all between the powerful one (al-qādir) and the necessitating cause (al-mūjib). Rather, the difference is that the conditions of influence in the case of the powerful one are quickly changing. When they are obtained after being absent, the powerful one becomes necessitating in influence; and when they cease after being present, he becomes impossible of influence. Except that this change is only conceivable for one whose effectivity depends on conditions separate from his essence. The Exalted Creator, before His influence on other than Him, does not depend on conditions separate from His essence, because He is the principle of everything other than Him. Therefore His influence on other than Him is not dependent on anything separate from Him. Consequently, His influence on other than Him is specific to His essence, and His essence is impossible of change, so His influence on other than Him is also impossible of change.

And this is the strong argument on which they rely and with which they triumph.
Source pages p. 124

Commentary
This passage is a proof presented by the philosophers (as quoted by Razi) to argue that if a free agent possesses all the preponderants for an action, the action becomes necessary, eliminating any real difference between a free agent and a necessitating cause. The argument proceeds in steps: 1) It defines the complete set of preponderants (perfect power, decisive will, time, instrument, benefit, removal of obstacles). 2) It considers whether refraining is possible when all are present. If refraining were possible, then the choice between act and refrain would require a further preponderant, leading to an infinite regress. 3) Therefore, the act must be necessarily obtaining when all preponderants are present, and necessarily absent when any condition is missing. 4) Hence, the agent becomes like a necessary cause, with the only difference being the fleeting nature of conditions in creatures. 5) But for God, whose essence is unchanging and independent of external conditions, His influence becomes eternally fixed as necessity. The passage concludes that this objection is the strongest one relied upon by the philosophers.

Key Terms
المرجحات
القادر
الموجب
الداعي
التسلسل
الترجيح

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That Razi endorses this argument as his own position.
Correction: Razi is presenting the philosophers' argument as a strong objection that he will later refute, not as his own view.

Checkpoint
According to the argument, when all preponderants are present, what is the status of the act?
A) The agent remains free to choose act or refrain.
B) The act becomes impossible.
C) The act becomes necessary and refrain impossible.
D) The agent becomes indifferent.

Key Takeaway
The argument forces a stark choice: either accept infinite regress or admit that when all preponderants are present, the act is necessary, collapsing the distinction between free agent and necessary cause.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you consider your own major decisions, do you find yourself assuming that once you have all reasons and desires aligned, you must act? Or do you believe you can still choose otherwise? How does this argument challenge your view of your own freedom?
?
If God's actions are eternally necessary from His essence, does that make you feel distant from a personal God who responds to your prayers, or does it give you confidence in His unchanging justice? How does your heart respond to the idea that God's influence is 'impossible of change'?
?
The argument concludes that for created agents like us, the difference between being a free agent and a necessitated cause is merely that our conditions change quickly. Look at a recent moment of hesitation in your life: was it a sign of incomplete preponderants, or a genuine choice? What does that reveal about your accountability before God?

The First Position: Action on Motive but Not Necessitation – First Critique
Imam al-Razi presents the first position and its first critique.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first mutakallimun position that divine action requires a motive but does not necessitate, and Imam al-Razi's first critique showing that this position leads to an impossibility.

Core Idea
If action is only 'more deserving' after the motive, it either becomes necessary or impossible.

Imam al-Razi begins his response to the philosophical objection by laying out the two main positions of the mutakallimun. The first position attempts to occupy a middle ground: the agent acts upon a motive, but the act does not become necessary. However, Razi immediately subjects this position to rigorous logical scrutiny. The first critique will reveal a fatal flaw in the concept of 'more deserving' without necessitation.

Guided Path
1
Read the first position carefully
Understand what the first mutakallimun claim: action depends on a motive, but the motive only makes the act 'more deserving' (awlā) without making it necessary. Notice how they try to preserve the difference between a necessitating cause and a voluntary agent.
The key term is 'awlā' – more deserving, not necessary.
2
Focus on the first critique
Imam al-Razi's first argument: At the time of equality between acting and refraining, preponderance is impossible. When one side becomes weaker (marjūḥ), its occurrence is even more impossible, forcing the other side to be necessary.
This directly challenges the notion of 'more deserving' without necessity.
3
Trace the logical steps
Follow the reasoning: if the act is 'more deserving', the opposite becomes 'less deserving' (weaker). But a weaker thing cannot occur over a stronger one; the stronger must occur. Therefore, the act becomes necessary, contradicting the position's premise.
Razi uses the principle of excluded middle: no third option between existence and non-existence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والجواب على السؤال الأول هو أن نقول : للمتكلمين في هذا المقام قولان: أحدهما : أن صدور الفعل عن القادر موقوف على الداعي إلا أن الفعل مع الداعي يصير أولى بالوقوع، إلا أنه لا ينتهي إلى حد الوجوب، فلأجل أنه صار أولى بالوقوع صار الوقوع راجحًا على اللاوقوع ولأجل أنه لا ينتهي إلى حد الوجوب يبقى الفرق بين الموجب والقادر. واعلم أن هذا الكلام ضعيف من وجهين : الأول وهو أن في الوقت الذي كان الفعل والترك في حيز التساوي كان رجحان الوجود على العدم في ذلك الوقت ممتنعاً، وعندما صار أحد الطرفين مرجوحا كان دخول المرجوح في الوجود حال كونه مرجوحا أولى بالامتناع؛ لأنه حال كونه مرجوحًا أضعف منه حال كونه مساوياً، وإذا كان دخول المرجوح في الوجود ممتنعا كان دخول الراجح في الوجود واجبًا ضرورة أنه لا خروج عن طرفي النقيض. والثاني : أن عند حصول كل مرجحات الوجود إما أن يكون العدم ممتنعا أو لا يكون، فإن كان ممتنعا كان الوجود واجبًا وهو المطلوب، وإن لم يكن العدم ممتنعا لم يلزم من فرض هذا العدم محال فلنفرض مع حصول كل مرجحات الوجود تارة حصول الوجود وأخرى حصول العدم، فاختصاص أحد الوقتين بحصول الوجود والوقت الثاني بحصول العدم إن لم يتوقف على مرجح مع أن نسبة كل تلك المرجحات إلى هذين الوقتين على السوية فقد ترجح الممكن المساوي على الآخر من غير مرجح، وهو محال، وإن توقف على انضمام مرجح إليه لم يكن الحاصل قبل ذلك كل المرجحات وكنا قد فرضنا حصول كل المرجحات، هذا خلف. ثم إنا ننقل التقسيم المذكور إلى هذه الحالة، أن بعد حصول هذا القيد وهو وهذا المرجح إن كان التأثير واجبًا فهو المقصود، و إن لم يكن واجبًا عاد التقسيم، وافتقرنا إلى قيد آخر ولزم التسلسل، أو الانتهاء إلى الوجوب، وهذا كلام قطع لا رجاء في دفعه. القول الثاني للمتكلمين في هذا المقام وهو أن صدور الفعل عن القادر لا يتوقف على انضمام الداعي والمرجح إليه، وهذا القول اختيار أكثر العلماء، و تقديره أن العطشان إذا خير بين شرب قدحين متساويين من جميع الوجوه فإنه يختار أحدهما على الآخر لا لمرجح وكذلك الجائع إذا خير بين أكل رغيفين متساويين من جميع الوجوه، وكذا الهارب من السبع الضاري إذا عنَّ له طريقان فإنه يختار أحدهما لا لمرجح ، فثبت أن صدور الفعل عن القادر لا يتوقف على الداعي. قالت الفلاسفة : الاعتراض على هذا الكلام من وجهين : الأول : أنه إذا جاز في العقل رجحان أحد طرفي الجائز على الآخر لا لمرجح أصلا لم يكن الاستدلال برجحان أحد طرفي الممكن على الطرف الآخر على وجود المرجح طريقا صحيحاً، وإذا كان لا سبيل إلى إثبات الصانع إلا بهذا الطريق ثم صار هذا الطريق مطعونا فيه لزم بطلان الاستدلال بالإمكان والحدوث على إثبات الصانع.
The answer to the first question is that we say: The mutakallimun have two statements in this context. One of them: That the occurrence of an act from the powerful (qādir) is dependent on the motive (dāʿī), except that the act with the motive becomes more deserving (awlā) of occurrence, yet it does not reach the limit of necessity (wujūb). So because it became more deserving of occurrence, occurrence becomes preponderant (rājiḥ) over non-occurrence; and because it does not reach the limit of necessity, the difference between the necessitating cause (mūjib) and the powerful (qādir) remains. Know that this statement is weak in two respects. The first: At the time when the act and refraining are in the sphere of equality, preponderance of existence over non-existence at that time is impossible. And when one of the two sides becomes preponderated (marjūḥ), the entry of the preponderated into existence while it is preponderated is more deserving of impossibility, because while it is preponderated it is weaker than while it is equal. And if the entry of the preponderated into existence is impossible, then the entry of the preponderant into existence is necessary, necessarily because there is no exit from the two extremes of the contradiction. The second: When all preponderating factors (murajjihāt) of existence are obtained, either non-existence is impossible or it is not. If it is impossible, then existence is necessary, which is the desired. And if non-existence is not impossible, then it does not follow from supposing this non-existence an impossibility. So let us suppose, with the attainment of all preponderating factors of existence, sometimes existence occurs and sometimes non-existence occurs. The specification of one time with the occurrence of existence and the second time with the occurrence of non-existence, if it does not depend on a preponderating factor while the relation of all those preponderating factors to these two times is equal, then the possible equal is preponderated over the other without a preponderating factor, which is impossible. And if it depends on the addition of another preponderating factor, then what was obtained before is not all the preponderating factors, and we had supposed the attainment of all preponderating factors. This is a contradiction. Then we transfer the mentioned division to this state: after the attainment of this condition (qayd) which is this preponderating factor, if the effect is necessary, that is the intent; and if it is not necessary, the division returns and we need another condition, leading to infinite regress (tasalsul) or termination at necessity. This is a decisive argument with no hope of refutation. The second statement of the mutakallimun in this context is that the occurrence of an act from the powerful does not depend on the addition of a motive and preponderating factor to it. And this statement is the choice of most scholars. Its explication is that if a thirsty person is given a choice between two equal glasses in all respects, he chooses one of them over the other without a preponderating factor. Similarly, a hungry person between two equal loaves, and likewise a person fleeing from a ferocious lion when two paths present themselves, he chooses one without a preponderating factor. Thus it is established that the occurrence of an act from the powerful does not depend on the motive. The philosophers said: The objection to this statement is in two respects. The first: If it is intellectually possible for one side of a contingent (jāʾiz) to be preponderated over the other without any preponderating factor at all, then inferring from the preponderance of one side of the possible over the other the existence of a preponderating factor is not a valid method. And if there is no way to prove the Creator except through this method, then this method becomes impugned, and the invalidity of inferring the Creator through contingency and origination (imkān wa al-ḥudūth) follows.
Source pages p. 125

Commentary
This passage is the answer to the first philosophical objection (which was not fully given in this window). It presents two positions of the mutakallimun regarding how a voluntary agent acts. The first position holds that action depends on a motive (dāʿī), but the motive only makes the action more deserving (awlā), not necessary. The author, Imam al-Razi, critiques this position with two arguments: First, at the moment of equality between action and refraining, preponderance is impossible; when one side becomes weaker (marjūḥ), its occurrence is even more impossible, forcing the other side to be necessary. Second, if all preponderating factors are present and non-existence is still possible, then the same factors could give different outcomes at different times, requiring either a new preponderating factor (leading to infinite regress) or termination at necessity. Thus the first position collapses. The second position, chosen by most scholars, denies that action depends on any motive or preponderating factor. Examples are given: a thirsty person choosing between two equal glasses, a hungry person between two loaves, and a fleeing person between two paths. The philosophers then object that if choice without a preponderating factor is possible, then the argument for God's existence from contingency (which relies on the need for a preponderating factor to explain why one possible state exists rather than another) becomes invalid. This objection is presented but not answered in this passage; it sets up a further discussion.

Key Terms

أولى

ضد النقيض

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The first position successfully distinguishes the powerful from the necessitating cause.
Correction: Imam al-Razi shows that the concept of 'more deserving' without necessitation is logically unstable, as it inevitably leads to necessity.

Checkpoint
According to Imam al-Razi's first critique, what happens when one side becomes preponderated (marjūḥ)?
A) The preponderated side becomes necessary.
B) The preponderated side's occurrence becomes more impossible, so the preponderant side becomes necessary.
C) Both sides remain possible.
D) The preponderated side becomes less deserving but still possible.

Key Takeaway
The first position's attempt to locate divine action between necessity and sheer possibility collapses under logical scrutiny.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you weigh a good deed against a sin, do you ever think the good is 'more deserving' but still choose the sin? According to al-Razi's critique, if one side is truly preponderant, it must occur. What does your hesitation reveal about the state of your heart? Will you today act on what you know is preponderant, regardless of your feelings?
?
Al-Razi shows that the concept of 'awlā' collapses into necessity when logically examined. How often do you use 'I'll try' or 'maybe' when you know the truth? Does your faith demand absolute certainty, or do you settle for 'more deserving'? What one area of your life have you been treating as 'more deserving' instead of 'necessary'? Commit now to treat it as a binding obligation before Allah.

The First Position – Second Critique: Infinite Regress or Necessity
Imam al-Razi delivers the second critique, a reductio ad absurdum.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Imam al-Razi's second critique of the first position, which shows that it leads to either an infinite regress of preponderating factors or an admission of necessity.

Core Idea
If all preponderating factors are present, non-existence must be impossible, or a new factor is needed indefinitely.

Imam al-Razi's second argument is even more decisive. It examines what happens when all preponderating factors are assumed to be present. Through a clever reductio, he shows that the first position cannot avoid either necessity or infinite regress. This argument is considered by Razi as 'cutting' with no hope of refutation.

Guided Path
1
Set up the scenario
Understand the assumption: all preponderating factors (murajjihāt) for the existence of the act are present. Under this condition, either non-existence is impossible or it is not.
This is a branching argument.
2
Follow the first branch
If non-existence is impossible, then existence is necessary. This already contradicts the first position that the act is not necessary.
3
Follow the second branch
If non-existence is not impossible, we can suppose that with all preponderating factors, sometimes existence occurs and sometimes non-existence occurs. This specification (why existence now and non-existence then) requires a preponderating factor.
4
Identify the dilemma
If the specification requires a new preponderating factor, then the original set was not 'all' factors, leading to contradiction. If no factor is needed, then preponderance without a preponderator occurs, which is impossible. This forces either an infinite regress of factors or a termination at necessity.
Razi claims this argument is decisive and unanswerable.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والجواب على السؤال الأول هو أن نقول : للمتكلمين في هذا المقام قولان: أحدهما : أن صدور الفعل عن القادر موقوف على الداعي إلا أن الفعل مع الداعي يصير أولى بالوقوع، إلا أنه لا ينتهي إلى حد الوجوب، فلأجل أنه صار أولى بالوقوع صار الوقوع راجحًا على اللاوقوع ولأجل أنه لا ينتهي إلى حد الوجوب يبقى الفرق بين الموجب والقادر. واعلم أن هذا الكلام ضعيف من وجهين : الأول وهو أن في الوقت الذي كان الفعل والترك في حيز التساوي كان رجحان الوجود على العدم في ذلك الوقت ممتنعاً، وعندما صار أحد الطرفين مرجوحا كان دخول المرجوح في الوجود حال كونه مرجوحا أولى بالامتناع؛ لأنه حال كونه مرجوحًا أضعف منه حال كونه مساوياً، وإذا كان دخول المرجوح في الوجود ممتنعا كان دخول الراجح في الوجود واجبًا ضرورة أنه لا خروج عن طرفي النقيض. والثاني : أن عند حصول كل مرجحات الوجود إما أن يكون العدم ممتنعا أو لا يكون، فإن كان ممتنعا كان الوجود واجبًا وهو المطلوب، وإن لم يكن العدم ممتنعا لم يلزم من فرض هذا العدم محال فلنفرض مع حصول كل مرجحات الوجود تارة حصول الوجود وأخرى حصول العدم، فاختصاص أحد الوقتين بحصول الوجود والوقت الثاني بحصول العدم إن لم يتوقف على مرجح مع أن نسبة كل تلك المرجحات إلى هذين الوقتين على السوية فقد ترجح الممكن المساوي على الآخر من غير مرجح، وهو محال، وإن توقف على انضمام مرجح إليه لم يكن الحاصل قبل ذلك كل المرجحات وكنا قد فرضنا حصول كل المرجحات، هذا خلف. ثم إنا ننقل التقسيم المذكور إلى هذه الحالة، أن بعد حصول هذا القيد وهو وهذا المرجح إن كان التأثير واجبًا فهو المقصود، و إن لم يكن واجبًا عاد التقسيم، وافتقرنا إلى قيد آخر ولزم التسلسل، أو الانتهاء إلى الوجوب، وهذا كلام قطع لا رجاء في دفعه. القول الثاني للمتكلمين في هذا المقام وهو أن صدور الفعل عن القادر لا يتوقف على انضمام الداعي والمرجح إليه، وهذا القول اختيار أكثر العلماء، و تقديره أن العطشان إذا خير بين شرب قدحين متساويين من جميع الوجوه فإنه يختار أحدهما على الآخر لا لمرجح وكذلك الجائع إذا خير بين أكل رغيفين متساويين من جميع الوجوه، وكذا الهارب من السبع الضاري إذا عنَّ له طريقان فإنه يختار أحدهما لا لمرجح ، فثبت أن صدور الفعل عن القادر لا يتوقف على الداعي. قالت الفلاسفة : الاعتراض على هذا الكلام من وجهين : الأول : أنه إذا جاز في العقل رجحان أحد طرفي الجائز على الآخر لا لمرجح أصلا لم يكن الاستدلال برجحان أحد طرفي الممكن على الطرف الآخر على وجود المرجح طريقا صحيحاً، وإذا كان لا سبيل إلى إثبات الصانع إلا بهذا الطريق ثم صار هذا الطريق مطعونا فيه لزم بطلان الاستدلال بالإمكان والحدوث على إثبات الصانع.
The answer to the first question is that we say: The mutakallimun have two statements in this context. One of them: That the occurrence of an act from the powerful (qādir) is dependent on the motive (dāʿī), except that the act with the motive becomes more deserving (awlā) of occurrence, yet it does not reach the limit of necessity (wujūb). So because it became more deserving of occurrence, occurrence becomes preponderant (rājiḥ) over non-occurrence; and because it does not reach the limit of necessity, the difference between the necessitating cause (mūjib) and the powerful (qādir) remains. Know that this statement is weak in two respects. The first: At the time when the act and refraining are in the sphere of equality, preponderance of existence over non-existence at that time is impossible. And when one of the two sides becomes preponderated (marjūḥ), the entry of the preponderated into existence while it is preponderated is more deserving of impossibility, because while it is preponderated it is weaker than while it is equal. And if the entry of the preponderated into existence is impossible, then the entry of the preponderant into existence is necessary, necessarily because there is no exit from the two extremes of the contradiction. The second: When all preponderating factors (murajjihāt) of existence are obtained, either non-existence is impossible or it is not. If it is impossible, then existence is necessary, which is the desired. And if non-existence is not impossible, then it does not follow from supposing this non-existence an impossibility. So let us suppose, with the attainment of all preponderating factors of existence, sometimes existence occurs and sometimes non-existence occurs. The specification of one time with the occurrence of existence and the second time with the occurrence of non-existence, if it does not depend on a preponderating factor while the relation of all those preponderating factors to these two times is equal, then the possible equal is preponderated over the other without a preponderating factor, which is impossible. And if it depends on the addition of another preponderating factor, then what was obtained before is not all the preponderating factors, and we had supposed the attainment of all preponderating factors. This is a contradiction. Then we transfer the mentioned division to this state: after the attainment of this condition (qayd) which is this preponderating factor, if the effect is necessary, that is the intent; and if it is not necessary, the division returns and we need another condition, leading to infinite regress (tasalsul) or termination at necessity. This is a decisive argument with no hope of refutation. The second statement of the mutakallimun in this context is that the occurrence of an act from the powerful does not depend on the addition of a motive and preponderating factor to it. And this statement is the choice of most scholars. Its explication is that if a thirsty person is given a choice between two equal glasses in all respects, he chooses one of them over the other without a preponderating factor. Similarly, a hungry person between two equal loaves, and likewise a person fleeing from a ferocious lion when two paths present themselves, he chooses one without a preponderating factor. Thus it is established that the occurrence of an act from the powerful does not depend on the motive. The philosophers said: The objection to this statement is in two respects. The first: If it is intellectually possible for one side of a contingent (jāʾiz) to be preponderated over the other without any preponderating factor at all, then inferring from the preponderance of one side of the possible over the other the existence of a preponderating factor is not a valid method. And if there is no way to prove the Creator except through this method, then this method becomes impugned, and the invalidity of inferring the Creator through contingency and origination (imkān wa al-ḥudūth) follows.
Source pages p. 125

Commentary
This passage is the answer to the first philosophical objection (which was not fully given in this window). It presents two positions of the mutakallimun regarding how a voluntary agent acts. The first position holds that action depends on a motive (dāʿī), but the motive only makes the action more deserving (awlā), not necessary. The author, Imam al-Razi, critiques this position with two arguments: First, at the moment of equality between action and refraining, preponderance is impossible; when one side becomes weaker (marjūḥ), its occurrence is even more impossible, forcing the other side to be necessary. Second, if all preponderating factors are present and non-existence is still possible, then the same factors could give different outcomes at different times, requiring either a new preponderating factor (leading to infinite regress) or termination at necessity. Thus the first position collapses. The second position, chosen by most scholars, denies that action depends on any motive or preponderating factor. Examples are given: a thirsty person choosing between two equal glasses, a hungry person between two loaves, and a fleeing person between two paths. The philosophers then object that if choice without a preponderating factor is possible, then the argument for God's existence from contingency (which relies on the need for a preponderating factor to explain why one possible state exists rather than another) becomes invalid. This objection is presented but not answered in this passage; it sets up a further discussion.

Key Terms
مرجحات
قيد
تسلسل
ترجيح بلا مرجح
خلف

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The first position can avoid infinite regress by stopping at the first motive.
Correction: The argument shows that whenever all factors are present and non-existence is still possible, a new factor is needed for each instance of existence, leading to infinite regress.

Checkpoint
What does Imam al-Razi conclude must happen if all preponderating factors are present and non-existence is not impossible?
A) Existence becomes impossible.
B) A new preponderating factor is required, leading to a contradiction or infinite regress.
C) The act becomes necessary immediately.
D) The agent's freedom is preserved.

Key Takeaway
The first position inevitably forces a choice between necessary causation and an infinite regress of motives.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you choose between two equally appealing options, do you pause to consider what tips the balance? Are you honest about the factors that influence you? Or do you pretend your choice is arbitrary while it is actually driven by a hidden preponderator? Commit to examining one significant decision today to identify its true determining factor.
?
If you believe your actions are not necessitated, how do you explain why you act one way at one time and differently at another, even when circumstances seem identical? This lesson argues that such differences require a preponderator. Could it be that you are unaware of the real motivator? Seek it out.
?
The argument ends by saying there is no hope of refutation. Do you accept that your free will may be limited by the need for sufficient reasons? How does that affect your understanding of your responsibility before Allah? Ponder on this in your next prayer.

The Second Position: Voluntary Action Without a Preponderating Factor
Imam al-Razi presents the second position and the philosophers' objection to it.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the second mutakallimun position that voluntary action does not require a motive or preponderating factor, and the philosophers' objection that this destroys the proof for God's existence.

Core Idea
If choice without a reason is possible, the cosmological argument from contingency fails.

The second position, favored by most scholars, asserts that a voluntary agent can act without any preponderating factor. Imam al-Razi provides intuitive examples from everyday choice. However, the philosophers immediately object that this undermines the very proof for God's existence, which relies on the principle that every contingent needs a preponderator. This lesson presents both the position and the weighty objection it faces.

Guided Path
1
Read the second position and examples
Understand the claim: a powerful agent (qādir) can act without a motive. Notice the examples: thirsty person, hungry person, fleeing person – all choose between equals without a reason.
This is the Ash'arite view on divine action.
2
Understand the philosophical context
The philosophers' argument for God's existence from contingency (imkān) relies on the principle that whenever one possible state exists rather than another, there must be a preponderating factor. If choice without a factor is allowed, this principle is false.
3
Read the philosophers' objection
The philosophers argue that if preponderance without a preponderator is possible, then the method of inferring a creator from contingency and origination becomes invalid.
This is a serious challenge to natural theology.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
This lesson reveals a tension between two fundamental Islamic teachings: that God is free to act without necessity, and that the universe's contingency points necessarily to a Creator. How does this tension sit with your own understanding of God? Do you rely more on rational arguments or on direct spiritual experience to affirm God's existence? What would change if the rational proof were invalidated in your mind?
?
The mutakallimun use everyday examples of choosing between equal options to defend divine freedom. Think of a moment when you made a choice without a clear reason—perhaps a decision that shaped your life. Do you believe that even that choice was ultimately determined by God's will? How does this lesson challenge you to see God's hand in the seemingly random events of your life?
?

Are you willing to face such challenges honestly, even if they unsettle your comfort? How can you strengthen your faith so that it is not solely dependent on one argument, but rooted in a living relationship with the Creator?

Second Argument: The Necessity of Existence When All Preponderants are Present
Razi presents a proof that existence must be necessary when all preponderating factors are present.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Razi's proof by contradiction that when all preponderating factors for existence are present, existence must be necessary, and how he eliminates the alternative possibilities.

Core Idea
When all causes for existence are present, nonexistence becomes impossible.

This lesson presents Razi's second argument, a rigorous proof that the presence of all preponderating factors necessitates existence. The argument proceeds by assuming the opposite and deriving a contradiction, either requiring an infinite regress or a preponderance without a preponderant factor. This is a foundational argument in Islamic theology for establishing causal necessity.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic and translation to grasp the flow of the argument. Notice the logical structure: if nonexistence is impossible, done; if not, we assume variability.
2
Understand the initial division
The argument splits into two main branches: either nonexistence is impossible (existence necessary) or it is not. Follow the branch where it is not impossible.
This is a proof by contradiction.
3
Identify the contradiction in the second branch
If nonexistence is not impossible, we can suppose existence at one time and nonexistence at another. The specification requires a preponderant factor, otherwise it's a preponderance without a preponderant (impossible). If it requires an additional preponderant, then the initial set wasn't complete, contradicting the assumption.
Both sub-branches lead to impossibility.
4
Grasp the transfer and infinite regress
Razi then transfers the division to the case with the added preponderant: either the effect is necessary (done) or we need another preponderant, leading to an infinite regress or eventual necessity.
This shows the argument is definitive.
5
Reflect on the conclusion
Razi calls this 'definitive speech with no hope of refuting it.' Consider why this argument is considered logically airtight.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والثاني: أن عند حصول كل مرجحات الوجود إما أن يكون العدم ممتنعا أو لا يكون، فإن كان ممتنعا كان الوجود واجبًا وهو المطلوب، وإن لم يكن العدم ممتنعا لم يلزم من فرض هذا العدم محال فلنفرض مع حصول كل مرجحات الوجود تارة حصول الوجود وأخرى حصول العدم، فاختصاص أحد الوقتين بحصول الوجود والوقت الثاني بحصول العدم إن لم يتوقف على مرجح مع أن نسبة كل تلك المرجحات إلى هذين الوقتين على السوية فقد ترجح الممكن المساوي على الآخر من غير مرجح، وهو محال، وإن توقف على انضمام مرجح إليه لم يكن الحاصل قبل ذلك كل المرجحات وكنا قد فرضنا حصول كل المرجحات، هذا خلف. ثم إنا ننقل التقسيم المذكور إلى هذه الحالة، أن بعد حصول هذا القيد وهو وهذا المرجح إن كان التأثير واجبًا فهو المقصود، و إن لم يكن واجبًا عاد التقسيم، وافتقرنا إلى قيد آخر ولزم التسلسل، أو الانتهاء إلى الوجوب، وهذا كلام قطع لا رجاء في دفعه.
The second: When all the preponderating factors of existence are present, either nonexistence is impossible or it is not. If it is impossible, then existence is necessary, which is the desired conclusion. And if nonexistence is not impossible, then from the assumption of this nonexistence no absurdity follows. So let us assume along with the presence of all preponderating factors of existence sometimes existence occurs and sometimes nonexistence occurs. The specification of one time with existence and the second time with nonexistence, if it does not depend on a preponderant factor while the ratio of all those preponderants to these two times is equal, then an equally possible thing would be preponderant over another without a preponderant factor, and that is impossible. And if it depends on the addition of a preponderant factor to it, then what was obtained before that was not all the preponderants, but we had assumed the presence of all preponderants; this is contradictory. Then we transfer the mentioned division to this case: after obtaining this condition (i.e., this preponderant factor), if the effect is necessary, then that is the intent; and if it is not necessary, the division returns, and we need another condition, leading to an infinite regress or to reaching necessity. And this is definitive speech with no hope of refuting it.
Source pages p. 126

Commentary
This passage is a proof by contradiction. It presents the second argument concerning the necessity of a preponderant factor. The argument assumes that all preponderating factors for existence are present. Then it considers two possibilities: either nonexistence is impossible, which means existence is necessary (as desired), or nonexistence is not impossible. If nonexistence is not impossible, one can suppose existence sometimes and nonexistence at other times. Since the preponderants are equally related to both times, the specification of one time for existence over the other would be a preponderance without a preponderant factor, which is impossible. Alternatively, if the specification depends on an additional preponderant factor, then the initial set was not complete, contradicting the assumption. This leads to an infinite regress or to a necessary effect, proving that existence must be necessary. Razi concludes that this argument is definitive and irrefutable.

Key Terms
مرجحات الوجود
العدم
الوجود الواجب
الممكن المساوي
التسلسل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: This argument is about free will and human choice.
Correction: The argument is purely about logical causality: when all preponderating factors are present, existence is necessary. It does not address human free will.

Checkpoint
According to the second argument, if all preponderating factors for existence are present and nonexistence is not impossible, what happens when we assume existence at one time and nonexistence at another?
A) The specification must be due to a preponderant factor, otherwise an impossibility occurs.
B) The assumption leads to a logical contradiction automatically.
C) The existence becomes necessary regardless.
D) The argument fails and we accept the possibility of preponderance without a preponderant factor.

Key Takeaway
Razi's argument proves that when all preponderating factors for existence are present, existence must be necessary; otherwise, we face logical absurdity or infinite regress.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In your own life, when all the conditions for a good deed are present — time, ability, means — do you still find yourself hesitating or failing to act? What missing preponderant in your heart does Razi’s argument force you to face? What is the real obstacle that makes nonexistence of the action possible despite all external factors being ready?
?
Think of a specific moment when you knew the truth and all circumstances favored it, yet you chose otherwise. According to Razi’s proof, if all preponderants were truly present, the outcome should have been necessary. What does that reveal about the incomplete state of your faith, your sincerity, or your fear of Allah? Will you today seek out that missing preponderant — whether it be duʿā’, repentance, or company of the righteous — so that your obedience becomes necessary?
?
Razi describes his argument as ‘definitive speech with no hope of refuting it.’ Yet your soul often wavers even when the truth is clear. How can you internalize this logical certainty so that your actions become a necessary consequence of your conviction? What practical step will you take this week to remove the internal preponderants of doubt, laziness, or attachment to this world that allow disobedience to persist?

Can an Act Occur Without a Preponderant Factor? The Mutakallimun's View
Razi presents the mutakallimun's view on voluntary action without a preponderant factor.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the rational argument that voluntary acts can occur without a specific preponderant factor, using everyday examples.

Core Idea
A capable agent can choose between equals without a motive.

This passage presents a key argument in Islamic theology about the nature of choice. The mutakallimun argue that a capable agent does not need an additional preponderant factor to act. They support this with intuitive examples of choosing between identical options.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Start by reading the Arabic passage and its English translation. Notice the concise style and the sequence from claim to examples to conclusion.
The passage is self-contained; focus on understanding the logical flow.
2
Identify the main claim
The first sentence states the opinion: the act of a capable agent does not depend on a motive or preponderant factor. This is the thesis.
3
Examine the three examples
The author provides three scenarios: a thirsty person choosing between equal cups, a hungry person choosing between equal loaves, and a person fleeing a beast choosing between equal paths. For each, the agent chooses one without a clear reason.
These examples are meant to be intuitively persuasive.
4
Understand the conclusion
The passage ends with 'thus it is established' – the examples are meant to prove the thesis. Reflect on why these examples support the claim.
The conclusion is a direct inference from the examples.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
القول الثاني للمتكلمين في هذا المقام وهو أن صدور الفعل عن القادر لا يتوقف على انضمام الداعي والمرجح إليه، وهذا القول اختيار أكثر العلماء، و تقديره أن العطشان إذا خير بين شرب قدحين متساويين من جميع الوجوه فإنه يختار أحدهما على الآخر لا لمرجح وكذلك الجائع إذا خير بين أكل رغيفين متساويين من جميع الوجوه، وكذا الهارب من السبع الضاري إذا عنَّ له طريقان فإنه يختار أحدهما لا لمرجح ، فثبت أن صدور الفعل عن القادر لا يتوقف على الداعي.
The second opinion of the mutakallimun in this context is that the occurrence of the act from the capable agent does not depend on the addition of the motive (al-dā'ī) and the preponderant factor (al-murajjih) to it. And this opinion is the choice of most scholars. Its exposition is that a thirsty person, when given the choice between drinking two equal cups from all aspects, chooses one over the other without a preponderant factor. Likewise, the hungry person, when given the choice between eating two equal loaves from all aspects, and similarly the one fleeing from a ferocious beast when two paths appear, chooses one without a preponderant factor. Thus it is established that the occurrence of the act from the capable agent does not depend on the motive.
Source pages p. 126

Commentary
This passage presents the second position of the mutakallimun regarding the necessity of a preponderant factor for voluntary action. It argues that the act of a capable agent (al-qādir) can occur without the addition of a motive or preponderant factor. This is the preferred view of most scholars. The proof is given through three examples: a thirsty person choosing between two identical cups of water, a hungry person choosing between two identical loaves of bread, and a person fleeing a predator choosing between two identical paths. In each case, the agent chooses one option without a specific preponderant factor, demonstrating that the act does not require a preponderant factor. The conclusion is that the occurrence of an act from a capable agent does not depend on the presence of a motive.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: This argument denies causality altogether, implying that actions have no cause.
Correction: The argument only rejects the necessity of an additional preponderant factor for free acts; it does not deny that the agent's own power and will are causes.

Checkpoint
What examples does the mutakallimun use to support the claim that the act of a capable agent does not require a preponderant factor?
A) A thirsty person choosing between equal cups, a hungry person choosing between equal loaves, and a fleeing person choosing between paths.
B) A person choosing between good and evil, a scholar choosing between two books, and a traveler choosing between two routes.
C) A sick person choosing medicine, a tired person choosing rest, and a wealthy person choosing charity.
D) A king choosing between two advisors, a judge choosing between two verdicts, and a merchant choosing between two goods.

Key Takeaway
The act of a capable agent does not require a preponderant factor; it can occur by mere choice, as shown by everyday examples of choosing between identical options.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

If your choices can occur without a preponderant factor, does that make you more or less accountable for them? Examine a past decision where you had no strong reason—are you willing to own that choice fully before Allah?
?
The mutakallimun used everyday examples to prove free choice. But do you live as if your choices are really free, or do you hide behind excuses like 'I had no other option'? What would change if you truly believed your actions depend only on your will?

Philosophers' Objection and the Mutakallimun's Response
Razi records the philosophers' objection and the mutakallimun's response.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the philosophers' objection that the mutakallimun's view on preponderance undermines the proof for God, and the mutakallimun's response distinguishing voluntary action from causal necessity.

Core Idea
The mutakallimun defend free will by distinguishing voluntary preponderance from causal necessity.

This passage presents a critical exchange between philosophers and mutakallimun. The philosophers argue that if preponderance without a cause is allowed, the classic proof for God's existence collapses. The mutakallimun respond by clarifying that their principle applies only to voluntary actions, not to the origination of contingent beings. Understanding this distinction is essential for appreciating the Islamic theological position on divine attributes and human free will.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation once without analysis. Let the full argument settle before interpreting.
Notice that the objection and response are each presented in a single block.
2
Identify the philosophers' objection
The philosophers claim that if preponderance without a cause is rationally permissible, then the inference from a possible thing's existence to a preponderant factor (God) is invalid. Find this reasoning.
This objection targets the standard proof from possibility and origination.
3
Examine the mutakallimun's response
The mutakallimun do not accept the objection as a blanket denial. They distinguish between two cases: a thing coming into existence after non-existence (requires a determinant) and a free agent choosing to act or refrain (does not require an efficient cause).
Pay attention to the terms 'حدوث' (origination) and 'إمكان' (possibility).
4
Understand the key distinction
The mutakallimun support their position by invoking the necessary distinction between voluntary power (القدر المختار) and necessitating cause (العلة الموجبة). Everyone naturally distinguishes human free will from natural phenomena like a falling stone.
This distinction is considered self-evident (ضروري).
5
Reflect on the conclusion
Any theoretical argument that eliminates the necessary distinction between voluntary and natural is false. Therefore, the philosophers' objection fails because it would destroy this necessary knowledge.
The mutakallimun affirm that the act of a capable agent does not depend on a preponderant factor.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قالت الفلاسفة : الاعتراض على هذا الكلام من وجهين : الأول : أنه إذا جاز في العقل رجحان أحد طرفي الجائز على الآخر لا لمرجح أصلا لم يكن الاستدلال برجحان أحد طرفي الممكن على الطرف الآخر على وجود المرجح طريقا صحيحاً، وإذا كان لا سبيل إلى إثبات الصانع إلا بهذا الطريق ثم صار هذا الطريق مطعونا فيه لزم بطلان الاستدلال بالإمكان والحدوث على إثبات الصانع. أجاب المتكلمون عن السؤال الأول بأنا لا نقول : إن رجحان أحد طرفي الممكن على الآخر لا يحوج إلى المرجح في جميع المواضع بل نقول : الشيء إذا وجد بعد عدمه فهذا الحدوث وهذا الإمكان هو المحوج إلى المقتضي، فأما ترجيح الفعل على الترك في حق القادر فذلك لا يحوج إلى المؤثر. والذي يدل عليه أن الفرق بين القدر المختار وبين العلة الموجبة أمر معلوم بالضرورة، فإن كل أحد يفرق بالضرورة بين كون الإنسان مختارًا في فعله وقوله و قیامه وقعوده وبين كون الحجر هابطا بالطبع والنار صاعدة بالطبع، وتوقف صدور الفعل عن القادر على المرجح يقتضي بأن يبقى بين الموجب و بين المختار فرق البتة، وكل نظري أفضى إلى فساد الضروري كان باطلا، فعلمنا أنه لا بد من الاعتراف بأن صدور الفعل عن القادر لا يتوقف على المرجح فهذا منتهى البحث في هذه الباب.
The philosophers said: The objection to this speech is from two aspects. First: If it is rationally permissible for one side of a contingent thing to be preponderant over the other without any preponderant factor at all, then the inference from the preponderance of one side of a possible thing over the other side to the existence of a preponderant factor would not be a valid method. And if there is no way to prove the Maker except through this method, and then this method becomes impugned, then the inference from possibility and origination to the proof of the Maker is invalid. The mutakallimun answered the first question: We do not say that the preponderance of one side of a possible thing over the other does not need a preponderant factor in all cases. Rather, we say: When a thing exists after its non-existence, this origination (al-ḥudūth) and this possibility are what necessitate a determinant (al-muqtaḍī). As for the preponderance of the act over omission on the part of the capable agent, that does not necessitate an efficient cause (al-mu'aththir). What indicates this is that the difference between the voluntary power (al-qadr al-mukhtār) and the necessitating cause (al-‘illa al-mūjiba) is necessarily known, for everyone necessarily distinguishes between a human being being free in his action, speech, standing, and sitting, and between a stone falling by nature and fire rising by nature. And making the occurrence of the act from the capable agent dependent on a preponderant factor would eliminate any difference between the necessitating and the voluntary. Every theoretical argument that leads to corruption of the necessary is false. So we know that it is necessary to acknowledge that the occurrence of the act from the capable agent does not depend on the preponderant factor. This is the end of the inquiry in this chapter.
Source pages p. 126

Commentary
This passage presents the philosophers' first objection and the mutakallimun's response. The philosophers argue that if preponderance without a preponderant factor is allowed, then the standard proof for the existence of God based on the preponderance of one possibility over another (such as existence over nonexistence) is invalidated. In response, the mutakallimun clarify that they do not deny the need for a preponderant factor in all cases. They distinguish between the origination of a thing from nonexistence to existence (which requires a determinant) and the free choice of a capable agent to act or refrain (which does not require an efficient cause). They support this by noting the necessary distinction between a voluntary agent and a natural cause: everyone naturally distinguishes between human free will and the involuntary motion of stones or fire. If the act of a capable agent depended on a preponderant factor, the voluntary would become indistinguishable from the necessitating, which contradicts necessary knowledge. Therefore, any theory leading to such contradiction is false, affirming the mutakallimun's position.

المرجح
الإمكان

العلة الموجبة
القدر المختار

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The mutakallimun claim that preponderance never needs a cause in any case.
Correction: They only say this for voluntary action; they affirm that origination of a thing requires a determinant.

Checkpoint
According to the mutakallimun's response, what necessitates the need for a determinant in the case of a thing's existence after non-existence?
A) The possibility of the thing.
B) The origination and possibility together.
C) The absence of a preponderant factor.
D) The nature of the capable agent.

Key Takeaway
The mutakallimun defend the proof for God by distinguishing between the need for a cause in origination and the absence of such need in voluntary action, preserving both rational theology and free will.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you truly believe that you have free will, or do you sometimes excuse your sins by feeling driven by circumstances or your environment? Recall a moment today when you chose between two options—was that choice truly yours, or did you feel compelled? How does this lesson's distinction between voluntary power and necessitating cause challenge you to take full ownership of your actions before Allah?
?
The philosophers argued that if preponderance without a cause is allowed, the proof for God collapses. Have you ever doubted God's existence because you couldn't logically prove Him? How does the mutakallimun's answer—that the proof holds for origination but not for voluntary acts—affect your confidence in the rational foundations of your faith? What would it mean for your relationship with Allah if you relied only on self-evident knowledge rather than complex arguments?
?
The mutakallimun say that any theory leading to corruption of necessary knowledge is false. Is there any belief you hold that contradicts something you know intuitively to be true? For example, do you truly live as though you are responsible for your choices, or do you secretly think your sins are predestined? How can you align your life with the self-evident truth that you are freely acting before Allah, and what would you need to change starting today?

The Philosophers' Empirical Objection and Response
Razi presents the philosophers' empirical objection and the beginning of the mutakallimun's response.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the philosophers' empirical objection that preponderance requires an inner inclination, and the mutakallimun's response invoking divine creation or celestial causes as the source of that inclination, while noting the argument's incomplete state.

Core Idea
Choice always requires an inner inclination, which must ultimately come from God or the heavens.

Razi presents the philosophers' challenge: even in self-observation, we find that choosing between two equal options requires an inner inclination (mayl). The philosophers argue that this inclination is a specific preponderant factor, proving that preponderance always requires a preponderant factor. They then address a possible counter-question about why the inclination occurs towards one option rather than another, invoking divine creation or celestial causes. However, the mutakallimun's full response is cut off mid-sentence.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyze. Then read the translation. Notice how the philosophers argue from self-observation.
The passage is cut off at the end; don't worry about incomplete sentences.
2
Identify the philosophers' claim
Find the example of the thirsty person with two cups. The philosophers say that without an inner inclination, no choice occurs. That inclination is a preponderant factor.
3
Understand the infinite regress argument
The philosophers anticipate an objection: what causes the inclination? They argue it cannot be another inclination (infinite regress). Instead, it must come from God or celestial causes.
This ties into the broader kalam debate about causality.
4
Note the incomplete response
The mutakallimun's answer to the second question is cut off. Identify where it breaks off and what might follow based on context.
The sentence ends with 'عند اختيار' suggesting a definition of the capable agent.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثاني : أنا إذا جربنا من أنفسنا في القدحين والرغيفين والطريقين علمنا أنه ما لم يحدث في قلبنا ميل وداعية إلى اختيار أحدهما دون الآخر، فإنا لا نختار ذلك المعين دون الآخر، أو إذا علمنا أنه لا بد في الترجيح من حصول الميل إلى أحدهما في القلب على التعيين فذلك الميل مرجح خاص فثبت أن في هذه الصورة لم يحصل الرجحان إلا مع المرجح. أقصى ما في الباب أن يقال : لا ندري لم حدث الميل إلى هذا الرغيف ولم يحدث إلى الرغيف الآخر ؟ لأنا نقول : ؛ سبب حدوث الميل في قلوبنا ليس لميل آخر في القلب، وإلا لزم التسلسل، بل الميول والإرادات تنتهي إلى ميل وإرادة تحدث في القلب إما بخلق الله تعالى أو بسبب من الأسباب السماوية، وحينئذ يكون هذا الإشكال زائلا. والذي يحقق هذا الكلام أن العطشان إذا خير بين القدحين فإنه ما لم يخص أحد القدحين بمد اليد لأخذه فإنه لا يمكنه أن يشرب ذلك الماء، وما لم يمل قلبه إلى أخذ ذلك القدح فإنه لا يمد يده إليه فذلك الميل الخاص والإرادة الخاصة مرجحة لأحد الطرفين على الآخر، فثبت أن في هذه الصورة لم يحصل الرجحان إلا المرجح ، وأما أنه لم حدث الميل إلى هذا ولم يحدث إلى ذلك ؟ فذلك مستند إلى الأسباب الفلكية. وأما الجواب عن السؤال الثاني: فهو أن ليس القادر عبارة عن الذي عند اختيار
Second: When we try it from ourselves regarding the two cups, the two loaves, and the two paths, we know that as long as there does not occur in our heart an inclination (mayl) and a motive (dā‘iya) to choose one over the other, we do not choose that specific one over the other. Or, if we know that in preponderance it is necessary for an inclination to occur towards one of them in the heart specifically, then that inclination is a specific preponderant factor. Thus it is established that in this scenario preponderance does not occur except with a preponderant factor. The most that can be said is: 'We do not know why the inclination occurred towards this loaf and did not occur towards the other loaf?' For we say: The cause of the occurrence of inclination in our hearts is not another inclination in the heart, otherwise an infinite regress would follow. Rather, inclinations and volitions end at an inclination and volition that occur in the heart either by creation of God Almighty or by some celestial cause. Then this problem would be removed. And what confirms this speech is that the thirsty person, when given the choice between the two cups, as long as he does not single out one cup by extending his hand to take it, he cannot drink that water. And as long as his heart does not incline towards taking that cup, he does not extend his hand towards it. So that specific inclination and specific volition are preponderant for one side over the other. Thus it is established that in this scenario preponderance does not occur except with a preponderant factor. As for why the inclination occurred towards this and did not occur towards that, that is due to celestial causes. As for the answer to the second question: it is that the capable agent is not the one who, when choosing...

Commentary
This passage presents the philosophers' second objection and the beginning of the mutakallimun's response, which is cut off. The philosophers argue from empirical self-observation: a thirsty person does not choose one of two equal cups without an inclination in the heart. This inclination is a specific preponderant factor, proving that preponderance always requires a preponderant factor. They then address a possible counter-question: why does the inclination occur towards one specific cup and not the other? They respond that the inclination is not caused by another inclination (to avoid infinite regress) but rather by divine creation or celestial causes. They illustrate with the thirsty person's need for both a hand extension and an inner inclination. Thus, they conclude that preponderance always involves a preponderant factor. The mutakallimun's response begins but is incomplete, only stating 'that the capable agent is not the one who, when choosing...'

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The philosophers are denying free will by claiming that inner inclinations determine choice.
Correction: The philosophers are not denying free will; they are arguing that choice always involves an inner inclination, which is a preponderant factor. They do not claim the inclination is compulsory; rather, it is a necessary condition for choice.

Checkpoint
According to the philosophers' second objection, what is necessary for the thirsty person to choose one of two equal cups?
A) An external preponderant factor like a reward.
B) An inclination in the heart towards that cup.
C) A rational deliberation comparing both cups.
D) The absence of any preponderant factor.

Key Takeaway
The philosophers argue that every choice requires an inner inclination, which must ultimately be caused by God or celestial bodies.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you are faced with a choice between two seemingly equal options, do you ever pause to recognize the inner inclination that tips the balance? The philosophers argue that this inclination is not self-generated but ultimately comes from Allah or celestial causes. How does this truth reshape your understanding of your own decisions? Will you consciously trace your inclinations back to Allah and thank Him for guiding your heart?
?
The passage reveals that every inclination in your heart is either created by Allah or caused by celestial forces. Think of a recent decision you made—perhaps a small one like what to eat or a major life choice. Can you pinpoint the moment when your heart inclined? Now consider: if that inclination came from Allah, why did He guide you that way? What does that tell you about your dependence on Him? Commit today to seeking His guidance (istikhara) before you allow your heart to settle on any choice, big or small.

The Eternal Cause and Temporal Effects: Razi's Response
Razi answers two objections against an eternal cause producing a temporal effect.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Razi defends the possibility of an eternal cause (Allah) producing temporal effects, addressing objections about causality and the specific timing of creation.

Core Idea
An eternal cause can produce temporal effects when prior states serve as conditions.

In this passage from Al-Arba'in, Imam Razi tackles a classic philosophical objection: how can an eternal being cause something that happens in time? The objector presents an alternative naturalistic explanation and raises two specific challenges. Razi's answers clarify key concepts in causality and the nature of time, providing a robust defense of Islamic theology.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing it. Then read the English translation to get a sense of the argument's flow.
Notice that this is a dialogue: the objector speaks, then Razi responds.
2
Identify the two parts of the objection
The objector first proposes an alternative naturalistic explanation (celestial motions), then raises two arguments: (1) a permanent essence cannot cause change, and (2) why was the world created at that specific time? Locate these in the text.
The two arguments are introduced by 'لا يقال : هذا مدفوع من وجهين'.
3
Focus on Razi's first answer
Razi explains that the eternal cause can produce a temporal effect because each prior state acts as a condition for the next. Grasp the idea of 'شرط' (condition) making causality possible across time.
This refutes the objection that a permanent thing cannot cause change.
4
Focus on Razi's second answer
Razi argues that since time itself began with the world, there is no 'before' to ask about. Understand why the question 'why was it not created earlier?' becomes meaningless.
This shows the incoherence of the objection when time is created.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قيل : لا نسلم أن تقدم المتأخر وتأخر المتقدم جائز، بيانه: أن من المحتمل أن تكون هذه الحوادث الأرضية مستندة إلى الاتصالات الفلكية، وتلك الاتصالات لازمة من كون كل واحد منها متحركًا على وجه خاص، وكون كل واحد منها متحركًا على وجه خاص إنما كان لأن ماهية كل واحد منها مخالف لماهية الآخر، فلا جرم كان كل واحد من تلك الماهيات قد استلزم نوعًا معينا من الحركات. لا يقال : هذا مدفوع من وجهين : الأول : أن القول بأن كل واحدة منها هي الموجبة لتلك الحركة باطل ؛ لأن تلك الماهية باقية، وتلك الحركة متغيرة والباقي لا يكون علة لغير الباقي. الثاني : أن هذا محتمل، ولكنا قد دللنا على حدوث العالم فلم اختص حدوث العالم بذلك الوقت ولم يحدث قبله ولا بعده ؟ لأنا نجيب عن الأول : بأنه لا يبعد أن يكون الدائم موجبًا للمتغير، على معنى أن كل حالة متقدمة سابقة فإنها تكون شرطًا لكون ذلك الباقي علة لوجود الحالة المتأخرة، وبهذا الطريق لا يمتنع كون الدائم علة للمتغير. وعن الثاني : أن بتقدير ثبوت القول بحدوث العالم وحدوث الزمان لم يكن قبل أول الزمان الحادث زمان آخر، وإذا كان كذلك لم يكن له قبل، وإذا لم يكن له قبل استحال أن يقال : لم لم يوجد قبله؟
If it is said: We do not accept that the priority of the posterior and the posteriority of the prior are possible. Its explanation: It is probable that these earthly events are based on celestial conjunctions, and those conjunctions are necessary from each of them being moving in a specific manner, and each of them being moving in a specific manner is only because the essence of each is different from the essence of the other; necessarily each of those essences necessitated a specific type of motion. It is not said: This is rebutted from two aspects: First: That the statement that each of them is the cause of that motion is false, because that essence is permanent, and that motion is changing, and the permanent cannot be a cause for the non-permanent. Second: That this is probable, but we have demonstrated the temporal origination of the world, so why was the temporal origination of the world specified for that time and did not occur before it nor after it? We answer the first: That it is not unlikely that the eternal is a cause for the temporal, meaning that each preceding state is a condition for that permanent being a cause for the existence of the subsequent state, and in this way it is not impossible for the eternal to be a cause for the temporal. And the second: That given the acceptance of the temporal origination of the world and the temporal origination of time, there was no other time before the first originated time; when this is so, it has no prior; when it has no prior, it is impossible to say: Why was it not before?
Source pages p. 147

Commentary
This passage addresses an objection against the possibility of an eternal cause creating a temporal effect. The objector first proposes an alternative naturalistic explanation involving celestial motions and essences, then raises two objections: (1) a permanent thing cannot be a cause of something changing, and (2) assuming the world is originated, why was it specified for that particular time? Razi answers the first by explaining that the permanent cause can produce temporal effects with prior states acting as conditions. For the second, he argues that since time itself began with the world, there is no 'before' to ask about.

تأخر
دائم
متغير
شرط

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: An eternal cause cannot produce a temporal effect because the effect must be simultaneous with the cause.
Correction: Razi explains that an eternal cause can produce a temporal effect when prior states act as conditions, allowing a chain of effects over time. Additionally, if time itself is created, questions about 'before' are invalid.

Checkpoint
According to Razi's answer to the second objection, why can we not ask why the world was not created earlier?
A) Because the world is eternal and has no beginning
B) Because time itself began with the world, so there is no 'before'
C) Because God's power is limited to specific times
D) Because the objector's premise is false

Key Takeaway
Razi demonstrates that an eternal cause can produce temporal effects by using prior states as conditions, and that questions about the specific timing of creation are invalid if time is created.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Razi argues that asking 'why not earlier' is meaningless if time itself is created. When you feel that Allah's answer to your dua is 'late', are you unconsciously assuming a timeline that Allah is not bound by? Will you tonight repent for that assumption and trust His wisdom?
?
Razi describes each prior state as a condition for the eternal cause to produce the next effect. What specific condition — a prayer, a charity, a repentance — might you be neglecting that delays the change you seek? What will you do about it today?
?
The eternal cause remains unchanged while effects unfold. Is there a sin you keep committing, thinking Allah's mercy has run out? How does Razi's model of an unchanging cause with changing conditions challenge you to see His mercy as always available, provided you fulfill the condition of sincere tawbah?
