Classification of Explanations for a Body's Continuance
Razi enumerates four possible determinants for a body's continuance and refutes two.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Razi's fourfold classification of possible determinants for a body's persistence and his refutations of two of them.

Core Idea
A body's continuance requires an explanation; Razi considers four possibilities and refutes two.

This passage dives into the theological debate about why created bodies continue to exist from moment to moment. Razi sets up the problem by establishing that bodies are 'possible of non-existence' and thus need a determinant. He then systematically enumerates four possible types of determinants and shows why two of them are untenable.

Guided Path
1
Establish the problem: possibility of non-existence
Read the first part to understand why a body, by its quiddity, is receptive to non-existence, thus requiring a determinant for its continuance.
This sets the stage for the classification.
2
Identify the four divisions
Find the four possible determinants: (1) agent does not annihilate, (2) absence of a contrary, (3) agent actively preserves, (4) something else.
The fourth is the correct view, to be discussed later.
3
Examine the refutation of the second division
Study the two proofs against the Mu'tazilite view that continuance is due to the absence of a contrary accident.
These proofs involve mutual opposition and circularity.
4
Understand why the third division is rejected
See the argument that active preservation amounts to 'bringing into existence what already exists,' which is impossible.
This is a common kalam argument against tahsil al-hasil.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المسألة الثامنة عشرة: يصير حدوثه يمتنع عليه العدم والدليل على كونه جائز العدم أن ماهيته قبل وجوده كانت موصوفة بالعدم، فثبت أن هذه الماهية قابلة للعدم وهذه القابلية من لوازم هذه الماهية فهذه الماهية قابلة للعدم أبدا، وهذا يقتضي كون الجسم جائز العدم في الزمان الثاني. المقدمة الثالثة لما ثبت أن الجسم حصل في الزمان الثاني مع جواز أن معدوما فالجائز لا بد له من المقتضي، وذلك المقتضي إما أن يكون أمرًا عدميًا أو وجوديًا، وعلى التقديرين فإما أن يكون مختارًا أو موجبًا، فهذه اقسام أربعة: القسم الأول: أن يقال: إنه ربما بقي لأن الفاعل المختار ما أعدمه، وهذا قول من قال: الإعدام بالقدرة جائز - وهو أحد قولي القاضي أبي بكر من أصحابنا وقول أبي الحسن الخياط من قدماء المعتزلة، وهو قول محمود الخوارزمي من متأخري المعتزلة - ومن الناس من أنكر ذلك، وقال: القدرة صفة مؤثرة، والعدم نفي محض، فإسناد العدم إلى القدرة محال. والقسم الثاني: أن يقال: إنه إنما بقي لأن له ضدّا متى وجد لزم من وجوده عدمه، وإذا لم يوجد ذلك الضد لزم بقاؤه، وهذا مذهب أبي علي وأبي هاشم من المعتزلة وزعموا أن ذلك الضد هو عرض لا في محل ويسمونه بالبقاء. وجمهور أصحابنا أبطلوا هذا القول من وجهين: الحجة الأولى: أن المضادة حاصلة من الجانبين فليس انقطاع الباقي لطريان الحادث أولى من اندفاع الحادث لوجود الباقي، وقالوا بل اندفاع الحادث لوجود الباقي أولى، وذلك لأن الحادث أضعف من الباقي بدليل أن الحادث لا يوجد إلا عند المقتضي، والباقي يبقى بدون المقتضي، وذلك يدل على أن الباقي أقوى. الحجة الثانية: أن طريان الضد الحادث مشروط بزوال الضد الباقي، فلو كان زوال الضد الباقي معللا بطريان الضد الحادث لزم الدور، وهو محال. القسم الثالث: أن يقال: إن الجوهر إنما بقي لأن الفاعل المختار يبقيه، قالوا: وهذا أيضًا محال وذلك لأن إيجاد الموجود وتحصيل الحاصل محال.
The eighth question: It becomes such that its occurrence prevents non-existence upon it. The proof that it is possible of non-existence is that its quiddity before its existence was described by non-existence. So it is established that this quiddity is receptive to non-existence, and this receptivity is among the concomitants of this quiddity, so this quiddity is always receptive to non-existence. This necessitates that the body is possible of non-existence in the second moment. The third premise: since it is established that the body came into existence in the second moment while it is possible that it be non-existent, then the possible necessarily requires a determinant. That determinant is either a non-existential matter or an existential matter, and on both suppositions it is either an agent of choice or a necessitating agent. So these are four divisions: The first division: It is said that perhaps it remained because the agent of choice did not annihilate it. This is the saying of those who said that annihilation by power is possible – and it is one of the two sayings of Judge Abu Bakr from our companions and the saying of Abu al-Hasan al-Khayyat from the early Mu'tazila, and it is the saying of Mahmud al-Khwarizmi from the later Mu'tazila – and among people there are those who denied that and said: Power is an effective attribute, and non-existence is pure negation, so attributing non-existence to power is impossible. The second division: It is said that it only remained because it has a contrary which, if it exists, necessarily results in its non-existence, and if that contrary does not exist, its remaining necessarily follows. This is the school of Abu 'Ali and Abu Hashim from the Mu'tazila, and they claimed that that contrary is an accident not in a locus and they call it baqa' (continuance). The majority of our companions invalidated this saying in two ways: The first proof: That opposition is from both sides, so the cessation of the remaining due to the occurrence of the originated is not more likely than the repulsion of the originated due to the existence of the remaining. Rather they said that the repulsion of the originated due to the existence of the remaining is more likely, because the originated is weaker than the remaining, evidenced by the fact that the originated only exists when there is a determinant, while the remaining remains without a determinant, and this indicates that the remaining is stronger. The second proof: That the occurrence of the originated contrary is conditional upon the removal of the remaining contrary, and if the removal of the remaining contrary were caused by the occurrence of the originated contrary, circularity would follow, and this is impossible. The third division: It is said that the substance only remained because the agent of choice preserves it. They said: This too is impossible, because bringing into existence what already exists and obtaining what has been obtained is impossible.
Source pages p. 186

Commentary
This passage presents a classification of possible explanations for why a body continues to exist in the second moment after it has come into existence, and then refutes two of those explanations. The author begins by establishing that a body is 'possible of non-existence' (ja'iz al-'adam) because its quiddity (mahiyya) was described by non-existence before existence, and that receptivity to non-existence is a concomitant of that quiddity. Hence, a body is always possible of non-existence. The third premise then states that whatever is possible requires a determinant (muqtadi) that brings it about or keeps it in existence. This determinant can be either non-existential or existential, and either from an agent of choice (mukhtar) or a necessitating agent (mujib). This yields four divisions. The first division is that the body remains simply because the agent of choice has not annihilated it. The author mentions that some, like Judge Abu Bakr and some Mu'tazila, hold that annihilation by power is possible, while others deny this because power is an effective attribute and non-existence is pure negation. The second division is that the body remains because it has a contrary (didd) that would annihilate it if it occurred; as long as that contrary does not occur, the body remains. The Mu'tazilites Abu 'Ali and Abu Hashim hold this view, and they call that contrary 'baqa'' (continuance), which they consider a non-locational accident. The majority of the author's companions (Ash'aris) refute this with two proofs. The first proof argues that opposition is mutual, so there is no reason to prefer the cessation of the existing over the repulsion of the new; in fact the existing is stronger because it persists without a determinant. The second proof shows that if the occurrence of the new contrary were the cause of the removal of the existing contrary, that would lead to circularity, which is impossible. The third division is that the agent of choice actively preserves the body. The author dismisses this as impossible because it would mean 'bringing into existence what already exists' (ijad al-mawjud), which is absurd. This leaves the fourth division, which will be discussed in the next lesson.

Key Terms
بقاء
جواز العدم
مقتضي
فاعل مختار
موجب
ضد
قدرة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that 'baqa' is simply the absence of annihilation, a passive state.
Correction: The passage shows that baqa' is a debated positive attribute; the Mu'tazilites consider it a contrary accident, and the Ash'aris refute that view.

Checkpoint
According to Razi's classification, which of the following is NOT one of the four possible determinants for a body's continuance?
A) The agent of choice did not annihilate it
B) A contrary accident exists that prevents annihilation
C) The agent of choice actively preserves it
D) A distinct accident within the body causes its continuance

Key Takeaway
Razi systematically classifies possible causes for a body's continuance and eliminates two options, leaving the way for a positive attribute of continuance (baqa').

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

If every moment of your existence requires a fresh act of preservation from Allah, how does that change your awareness of your dependence on Him right now?
?
You have just learned that your own continuance is not self-sustaining but needs a determinant. When was the last time you truly felt that your next heartbeat depends entirely on Allah's will?
?
The passage shows theologians debating why a body persists. Do you live as if your life is guaranteed, or do you wake each day knowing it is a new gift requiring a fresh decision from the Creator?

Razi's Proof for Baqa' as a Distinct Accident
Razi refutes common accidents as causes of continuance and asserts baqa' as a distinct accident.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Razi argues that the continuance of a substance requires a distinct accident called baqa', and why common accidents cannot cause continuance.

Core Idea
Continuance is a distinct effect requiring a distinct cause.

Razi continues his analysis of the possible causes for a substance's continued existence. Having eliminated other options, he now examines the fourth division: that the substance continues because accidents in it necessitate continuance. He then refutes the idea that these accidents could be the well-known ones, establishing the need for a separate accident of baqa'.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic source passage carefully, then read the literal translation. Get a sense of Razi's argumentative flow.
The passage is a compact logical argument; take your time.
2
Identify the two options
Notice that Razi presents two possibilities: either the accident causing continuance is one of the well-known accidents or it is a distinct accident. He then refutes the first option.
The well-known accidents include colors, tastes, smells, wills, powers, etc.
3
Understand the two arguments
Razi gives two reasons why well-known accidents cannot cause continuance: first, each has a unique effect (a single cause cannot produce two different effects); second, if one could cause continuance, there would be no grounds to choose which one, leading to multiple causes for a single effect.
Both arguments rely on the principle that causes have specific effects.
4
Grasp the conclusion
After eliminating the first option, Razi concludes that the only remaining explanation is that continuance is caused by an accident additional to the well-known ones, which is called baqa'.
Baqa' is thus established as a distinct attribute necessary for the continued existence of substances.
5
Test your understanding
Answer the checkpoint question to verify your comprehension of the key logical move in the passage.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والقسم الرابع : وهو أنه إنما بقي لأنه قام بالجوهر في الزمان الثاني أعراض مقتضية لبقاء ذلك الجوهر، ثم نقول : ذلك العرض إما أن يكون هو من الأعراض المشهورة - وهي الألوان والطعوم والروائح والإرادات والقدر وأمثالها - وإما أن يكون عرضا زائدا عليها مغايرا لها. والأول باطل لوجهين : أحدهما : أن لكل واحد من هذه الأعراض حكمًا خاصًا، فالحركة توجب المتحركية، والعلم يوجب العالمية، فلو اقتضت أيضًا كون الجوهر باقيا لزم أن تصدر العلة الواحدة حكمان مختلفان، وذلك محال. والثاني : هو أنه ليس تعليل بقاء الجوهر ببعض هذه الأعراض أولى من تعليل بقائه بالباقي فيلزم أن يكون بقاؤه معللا بكل هذه الأعراض، فيلزم تعليل الحكم الواحد بالعلل الكثيرة، وهو محال، ولما بطلت كل هذه الأقسام ولم يبق إلا أن يعلل استمرار الجوهر بعرض زائد على هذه الأعراض وجب الاعتراف به، وذلك هو البقاء.
The fourth division: That it only remained because accidents subsisting in the substance in the second moment necessitate the continuance of that substance. Then we say: That accident is either from the well-known accidents – which are colors, tastes, smells, wills, powers, and the like – or it is an accident additional to them, different from them. The first is invalid for two reasons: One of them: That each of these accidents has a specific ruling; motion necessitates being-moved, knowledge necessitates being-knowing. So if it also necessitated that the substance be remaining, then a single cause would produce two different effects, and that is impossible. The second: That it is not more appropriate to attribute the continuance of the substance to some of these accidents than to the rest, so its continuance would have to be attributed to all these accidents, necessitating the attribution of a single effect to multiple causes, and that is impossible. When all these divisions have been invalidated and nothing remains except to explain the continuation of the substance by an accident additional to these accidents, it is necessary to acknowledge it, and that is baqa' (continuance).
Source pages p. 186

Commentary
This passage completes the classification by examining the fourth division in detail. The fourth division proposes that the substance continues because accidents subsist in it that necessitate its continuance. These accidents could be either the well-known accidents (such as colors, tastes, etc.) or a distinct accident called baqa'. The author first refutes the idea that common accidents can cause continuance. Two arguments are given. First, each well-known accident has a specific effect (e.g., motion causes being-moved, knowledge causes being-knowing). If such an accident also caused the substance to be remaining, then a single cause would produce two different effects, which is impossible (a cause cannot produce multiple distinct effects in a single subject). Second, if one of these accidents were the cause of continuance, there would be no reason to choose one over another, so all would be equally valid causes; that would mean a single effect (continuance) is produced by multiple independent causes, which is also impossible. Therefore, the only remaining option is that the accident causing continuance is an accident additional to the well-known ones, and that is what the author calls baqa' (continuance). Thus, the argument establishes baqa' as a distinct attribute necessary for the continued existence of contingent substances.

Key Terms
بقاء
عرض
أعراض مشهورة
علة
معلول

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That a substance's continued existence does not require any additional cause; it simply persists on its own.
Correction: Razi shows that continuance is an effect that requires a cause, and that cause is a distinct accident called baqa', not the absence of annihilation.

Checkpoint
Why does Razi reject the idea that a common accident like 'knowledge' can also cause the continuance of a substance?
A) Knowledge is too weak to sustain existence
B) Each accident has a single specific effect, so a second effect is impossible
C) Knowledge is not permanent enough
D) Knowledge only exists in living beings

Key Takeaway
The continuance of a substance is an effect that requires a distinct cause, which Razi identifies as the accident of baqa'.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If every moment of your existence is dependent on a new accident of baqa' created by God, do you truly feel that dependence in your daily life, or do you act as if your existence is self-guaranteed?
?
How does recognizing that even your continued existence requires a distinct causal act from God change the way you approach times when you feel your life is slipping away—illness, aging, or danger?
?
Razi argues that no well-known accident (like knowledge or power) can cause continuance—only a distinct accident from God. What does this imply about your reliance on your own abilities or qualities to sustain you? Are you trusting in them instead of the One who gives you existence each moment?

Objection and Response: Baqa' as Cause of Essence or Attribute?
Razi presents an objection that baqa' cannot cause the essence and responds by reinterpreting baqa' as cause of the attribute of remaining.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the kalam objection that baqa' cannot be a cause of the essence of a substance and the response that baqa' is a cause of the attribute of remaining, not the essence itself.

Core Idea
Baqa' causes the attribute of remaining, not the essence itself.

In kalam discussions on accidents, the status of baqa' as an accident is debated. Here, Razi presents an objection against those who claim baqa' is a cause of the substance's essence. He then provides a nuanced response that clarifies baqa' is actually a cause of the renewed attribute of 'being-remaining'. This distinction resolves potential logical absurdities.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic and translation. Notice the structure: first the objection with three points, then the response.
Focus on the logical flow from objection to resolution.
2
Identify the three objections
List the three reasons why baqa' cannot cause the essence: 1) tahsil al-hasil, 2) renewal of cause necessitates renewal of effect, 3) circularity.
Each objection targets a different logical problem.
3
Understand the response
The response reinterprets baqa' as cause not of the essence but of the attribute of remaining, which is a renewed judgment.
This move avoids the absurdities by shifting the causal target.
4
Reflect on the resolution
See how the response resolves each objection and allows the original argument for baqa' to stand.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والجواب : أنتم لما أثبتم البقاء لا يمكنكم أن تقولوا إن البقاء علة لذات الجوهر، ويدل عليه وجوه : الأول : أن ذات الجوهر كان موجودًا قبل ذلك، فلو كان البقاء علة له لكان هذا تحصيلا للحاصل وهو محال. والثاني : أن البقاء لا يبقى فلو كان البقاء علة لذات الجوهر لزم من تجدد العلة تجدد المعلول، فيلزم أن لا يبقى الجوهر في الزمان الثاني. الثالث: هو أن البقاء عرض مفتقر إلى الجوهر، فلو كان الجوهر معللا به لزم الدور، وهو محال، وأنتم إذا أثبتم البقاء لا بد أن تقولوا : أنه علة لا لذات الجوهر بل لكونه باقيًا، وهو حكم متجدد زائد على الذات، وإذا كان الأمر كذلك فلم لا يجوز إسناد هذا الحكم المتجدد إلى الفاعل المختار وعلى هذا التقدير لا يلزم إيجاد الموجود لأن الواقع بالفاعل المختار هو هذا الحكم المتجدد لا أصل الذات، وعلى هذا التقدير يسقط ما ذكرتموه من الاستدلال.
The answer: Since you have established baqa', you cannot say that baqa' is a cause of the essence of the substance. This is indicated by several aspects: The first: That the essence of the substance was existent before that, so if baqa' were a cause for it, that would be obtaining what has been obtained, and that is impossible. The second: That baqa' does not remain, so if baqa' were a cause of the essence of the substance, then the renewal of the cause would necessitate the renewal of the effect, and it would follow that the substance does not remain in the second moment. The third: That baqa' is an accident dependent on the substance, so if the substance were caused by it, circularity would follow, and that is impossible. But when you establish baqa', you must say that it is a cause not of the essence of the substance but of its being-remaining, which is a renewed judgment additional to the essence. If this is the case, then why is it not permissible to attribute this renewed judgment to the agent of choice? On this assumption, bringing into existence what already exists does not follow, because what occurs by the agent of choice is this renewed judgment, not the origin of the essence. On this assumption, the inference you have mentioned collapses.
Source pages p. 187

Commentary
This passage is a dialectical objection and response. The objector (likely a proponent of the view that baqa' is a distinct accident) argues that those who affirm baqa' cannot claim that baqa' is a cause of the essence of the substance itself, because that would entail several absurdities. First, the essence already exists, so making it a cause would be 'obtaining what has been obtained' (tahsil al-hasil), which is impossible. Second, baqa' itself is not permanent; it is renewed at each moment. If it were the cause of the essence, then each renewal of baqa' would necessitate a new essence, meaning the substance would not persist as the same entity. Third, baqa' is an accident that depends on the substance for its existence; if the substance were caused by baqa', that would be circular (the cause depends on its effect). The response is to clarify that baqa' is not a cause of the essence itself but of the attribute of 'being-remaining' (kawnihi baqiyan), which is a renewed judgment (hukm mutajaddid) distinct from the essence. This renewed judgment can be attributed to the agent of choice without implying re-creation of the essence. Therefore, the objection is resolved, and the earlier argument for baqa' stands.

Key Terms

علة
ذات
تحصيل الحاصل
دور
حكم متجدد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Baqa' is a cause of the essence of the substance.
Correction: Baqa' is actually a cause of the attribute of remaining, not the essence itself.

Checkpoint
In the objection, why is it impossible for baqa' to be a cause of the essence of the substance?
A) Because baqa' is not an accident
B) Because the essence already exists, so causing it would be 'obtaining what has been obtained'
C) Because baqa' is eternal
D) Because the substance does not need baqa'

Key Takeaway
Baqa' causes the renewed attribute of remaining, not the essence, thus avoiding the logical problems of tahsil al-hasil, renewal, and circularity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
This lesson shows that your continued existence each moment is a 'renewed judgment' from Allah. When you woke up today, did you think of yourself as being kept in existence by a fresh act of divine will? How would your morning have been different if you had truly felt that dependency?
?
The objection of 'obtaining what has already been obtained' warns against presuming upon God as if His previous actions secure the future. Is there an area of your life—spiritual, relational, or material—where you are living off past grace without seeking renewal? What is one specific step you will take today to re-submit that area to Allah's ongoing choice?
?
If a mere accident like baqa' cannot cause the essence, how much more must you, a mere creature, rely on Allah's direct causality for every breath? Do you find that truth liberating or unsettling? In what way will you consciously acknowledge Allah's direct involvement in your next action, however small?

The Apparent Necessity of Permanence as an Attribute
Razi presents two arguments for the necessity of an attribute of permanence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two arguments that seem to require an additional attribute of permanence for substances, and why they appear compelling.

Core Idea
One cause cannot have two effects; multiple causes cannot have one effect – so permanence must be a separate attribute.

In this passage, Imam al-Razi first presents the reasoning that drives some theologians to posit 'al-baqa'' as an accident superadded to the substance's essence. He gives two logical arguments that appear to show that the substance's persistence cannot be explained by other accidents, nor by multiple accidents, thereby seemingly necessitating a distinct attribute. Understanding these arguments is crucial before examining Razi's refutation.

Guided Path
1
Read the first argument
Each accident has a specific judgment (e.g., motion gives being-moving). If the same accident also gave permanence, one cause would have two effects, which is impossible.
This argument relies on the principle of causal uniqueness.
2
Read the second argument
It is arbitrary to attribute permanence to one accident over another, so if any could, all would, leading to multiple causes for one effect, also impossible.
This argument uses the principle of indifference.
3
Notice the conclusion
Therefore, it seems we must affirm a separate accident called permanence. This sets up the need for refutation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والأول باطل لوجهين :
أحدهما : أن لكل واحد من هذه الأعراض حكمًا خاصًا، فالحركة توجب المتحركية، والعلم يوجب العالمية، فلو اقتضت أيضًا كون الجوهر باقيا لزم أن يصدر العلة الواحدة حكمان مختلفان، وذلك محال.
والثاني : هو أنه ليس تعليل بقاء الجوهر ببعض هذه الأعراض أولى من تعليل بقائه بالباقي فيلزم أن يكون بقاؤه معللا بكل هذه الأعراض، فيلزم تعليل الحكم الواحد بالعلل الكثيرة، وهو محال، ولما بطلت كل هذه الأقسام ولم يبق إلا أن يعلل استمرار الجوهر بعرض زائد على هذه الأعراض وجب الاعتراف به، وذلك هو البقاء.
والجواب : أنتم لما أثبتم البقاء لا يمكنكم أن تقولوا إن البقاء علة لذات الجوهر، ويدل عليه وجوه :
الأول : أن ذات الجوهر كان موجودًا قبل ذلك، فلو كان البقاء علة له لكان هذا تحصيلا للحاصل وهو محال.
والثاني : أن البقاء لا يبقى فلو كان البقاء علة لذات الجوهر لزم من تجدد العلة تجدد المعلول، فيلزم أن لا يبقى الجوهر في الزمان الثاني.
الثالث: هو أن البقاء عرض مفتقر إلى الجوهر، فلو كان الجوهر معللا به لزم الدور، وهو محال، وأنتم إذا أثبتم البقاء لا بد أن تقولوا : أنه علة لا لذات الجوهر بل لكونه باقيًا، وهو حكم متجدد زائد على الذات، وإذا كان الأمر كذلك فلم لا يجوز إسناد هذا الحكم المتجدد إلى الفاعل المختار، وعلى هذا التقدير لا يلزم إيجاد الموجود لأن الواقع بالفاعل المختار هو هذا الحكم المتجدد لا أصل الذات، وعلى هذا التقدير يسقط ما ذكرتموه من الاستدلال.
And the first is false for two reasons: One: each of these accidents has a specific judgment, so motion necessitates being-moving, and knowledge necessitates being-knowing. So if it also necessitated the substance being permanent, then a single cause would produce two different judgments, and that is impossible. Two: it is not that explaining the permanence of the substance by some of these accidents is more appropriate than explaining its permanence by the rest, so it would follow that its permanence is explained by all these accidents, thus explaining a single judgment by multiple causes, which is impossible. When all these categories are invalidated, and nothing remains except that the continuation of the substance is explained by an accident additional to these accidents, it becomes necessary to affirm it, and that is 'al-baqa'' (permanence). The answer: Since you have affirmed permanence, you cannot say that permanence is a cause for the essence of the substance. This is indicated by several aspects: First: The essence of the substance existed before it, so if permanence were a cause for it, this would be a bringing-about of something already existent, which is impossible. Second: Permanence does not itself endure; so if permanence were a cause for the substance's essence, the renewal of the cause would necessitate the renewal of the effect, and thus the substance would not remain in the second moment. Third: Permanence is an accident that depends on the substance, so if the substance were caused by it, a circularity would result, which is impossible. And when you affirm permanence, you must say that it is a cause not for the essence of the substance but for its being permanent, which is a renewed judgment additional to the essence. If this is the case, then why is it not permissible to attribute this renewed judgment to the free agent (al-fāʿil al-mukhtār)? On this assumption, bringing about something existent is not entailed, because what occurs by the free agent is this renewed judgment, not the essence itself. On this assumption, your argument collapses.
Source pages p. 187

Commentary
This passage is a refutation within a larger discussion about whether the permanence (baqa') of a substance (jawhar) requires a superadded attribute. The text begins by presenting two arguments that make it appear that baqa' must be an additional accident. First, each accident has a specific effect, so if the same accident also caused permanence, one cause would have two effects, which is impossible. Second, it is arbitrary to single out one accident as the cause of permanence; therefore, if any accident could cause it, all would, leading to multiple causes for one effect, also impossible. Thus, it seems necessary to posit a distinct accident called baqa'. Then the author (Razi) gives the answer (al-jawab), refuting that baqa' can be a cause of the substance's essence. He presents three arguments: (1) The substance's essence already exists before permanence, so making permanence a cause would be trying to bring about what already exists (taḥṣīl al-ḥāṣil). (2) Permanence itself does not endure, so if it were a cause, its renewal would cause the substance to cease each moment. (3) Permanence depends on the substance, so if the substance were caused by permanence, there would be circular dependence (dawr). Razi then clarifies that the proponents of baqa' actually claim it is a cause for the substance's being permanent (i.e., its persistence), not for its essence. He questions why this renewed judgment cannot be directly attributed to the free agent (God or nature?) without an additional attribute. This argument shows that positing baqa' as an attribute of the substance leads to absurdities, and thus the need for such an attribute is undermined.

Key Terms
بقاء
جوهر
عرض
علة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: These two arguments conclusively prove the existence of an attribute of permanence.
Correction: Razi will later show that these arguments fail and that the concept of an attribute of permanence leads to contradictions.

Checkpoint
What is the first argument against the view that permanence can be explained by other accidents?
A) It would lead to circularity.
B) A single cause cannot produce two different effects.
C) Permanence itself is not permanent.
D) There is no difference between the accidents.

Key Takeaway
The opponent's case for a distinct attribute of permanence rests on the impossibility of one cause having multiple effects and the indeterminacy of which accident would cause it, but these arguments are not decisive.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you think about your own beliefs, do you ever assume that one reason or cause can explain multiple outcomes? How does the principle of 'one cause, one effect' in this passage challenge you to re-examine where you might be oversimplifying or misattributing causality in your life?
?
The opponent's argument seems logical, yet Razi will refute it. Have you ever clung to a logical argument for a religious belief, only to later realize it was flawed? How do you react when the foundations of your reasoning are shaken? This week, deliberately seek out one of your own assumptions and test it against revelation.
?
If the permanence of a substance cannot be explained by its own accidents, then what does that hint about the necessity of a Creator who sustains everything? In what ways are you relying on 'created permanence' (like your health, wealth, or status) rather than on the Eternal One who alone grants continuity? Commit today to naming one area where you need to shift your trust.

Razi's Refutation: Three Arguments Against Permanence as a Cause
Razi gives three refutations and a final objection against the attribute of permanence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Razi's three counter-arguments against the claim that permanence is a cause for the substance's essence, and his final point attributing persistence to the free agent.

Core Idea
Permanence cannot cause the substance's essence – otherwise we would have tahsil al-hasil, renewal of cause, or circularity.

After presenting the arguments that seem to require an attribute of permanence, Razi now refutes the claim that this permanence attribute can be a cause for the substance's essence. He offers three devastating logical arguments: that it would entail bringing about what already exists, that permanence itself does not endure, and that it would lead to circular dependence. He then concludes that persistence can be directly attributed to the free agent without any additional attribute.

Guided Path
1
Read the first argument
The essence exists before permanence, so permanence as a cause would be tahsil al-hasil (bringing about the already existent).
2
Read the second argument
Permanence itself does not endure, so if it were a cause, its renewal would cause the substance to cease each moment.
3
Read the third argument
Permanence depends on the substance, so the substance depending on permanence would be circular.
4
Understand the final point
The opponents actually claim permanence is a cause for the substance's being permanent, not its essence. Razi asks why this renewed judgment cannot be directly from the free agent.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والأول باطل لوجهين :
أحدهما : أن لكل واحد من هذه الأعراض حكمًا خاصًا، فالحركة توجب المتحركية، والعلم يوجب العالمية، فلو اقتضت أيضًا كون الجوهر باقيا لزم أن يصدر العلة الواحدة حكمان مختلفان، وذلك محال.
والثاني : هو أنه ليس تعليل بقاء الجوهر ببعض هذه الأعراض أولى من تعليل بقائه بالباقي فيلزم أن يكون بقاؤه معللا بكل هذه الأعراض، فيلزم تعليل الحكم الواحد بالعلل الكثيرة، وهو محال، ولما بطلت كل هذه الأقسام ولم يبق إلا أن يعلل استمرار الجوهر بعرض زائد على هذه الأعراض وجب الاعتراف به، وذلك هو البقاء.

And the first is false for two reasons: One: each of these accidents has a specific judgment, so motion necessitates being-moving, and knowledge necessitates being-knowing. So if it also necessitated the substance being permanent, then a single cause would produce two different judgments, and that is impossible. How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Razi shows that attributing the persistence of creation to a necessary cause leads to absurdities. In your daily life, do you unconsciously attribute your stability—your health, provision, or faith—to secondary causes as if they were necessary, rather than to the direct will of the Free Agent? How would your reliance on Allah change if you truly internalized that He alone sustains you moment by moment?
?
The argument of circularity (دور) exposes the danger of assuming that what depends on you can also be the foundation of your existence. Is there any belief, relationship, or habit in your life that functions as a 'circular' support—you depend on it, but it also depends on you? Will you cut that cycle and ground your life solely on the Self-Sufficient?
?
Razi concludes that the renewed judgment of 'being permanent' can be directly from the Free Agent, without needing an intermediate attribute. Do you live as if your obedience or worship today is sustained by Allah's direct action, or do you secretly rely on your own past achievements? What would it look like to seek His direct renewal of your faith every single moment?

Proof that Distinction Entails Commonality
Imam Razi eliminates three of four possibilities and concludes that the distinguishing cause entails the common cause.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Imam Razi argues that the aspect of distinction must entail the aspect of commonality, and how this logical proof supports the principle that similar effects require different causes.

Core Idea
Distinction logically entails commonality, not the reverse.

Imam Razi continues his discussion of causation by examining the relationship between what makes things alike and what makes them distinct. He presents a logical elimination argument, showing that only one of four possible relations is coherent. This lesson walks through each possibility and why only the fourth survives.

Guided Path
1
Understand the premise
The passage begins by stating that the aspect of similarity and the aspect of distinction are different. This is crucial for the subsequent logic.
2
List the four possibilities
Imam Razi enumerates four possible entailment relations between these two aspects. Read them carefully.
The divisions are: no entailment, mutual entailment, similarity entails distinction, distinction entails similarity.
3
Follow the elimination
The passage then eliminates the first three divisions by showing they lead to contradictions. Pay attention to why each fails.
The first eliminates because it would prevent a single reality; the second and third because they would make distinction ineffective.
4
Grasp the conclusion and implication
Only the fourth division remains: distinction entails similarity. This forces the conclusion that equal things must be caused by different causes.
Consider how this connects to the broader kalam discussion on causality.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أن ما به التشابه مغاير لما به الامتياز. إذا ثبت هذا فنقول : إما أن لا تكون بين الاعتبارين ملازمة أصلا، وإما أن يكون كل واحد منهما مستلزما للآخر، وإما أن يكون ما به المشاركة مستلزما لما به الممايزة، وإما أن يكون بالعكس. والقسم الأول باطل، وإلا لانفك كل واحد من هذين الاعتبارين عن الآخر، وحينئذ لا يحصل من مجموعهما حقيقة واحدة هذا خلف والثاني والثالث أيضًا باطل، إذ لو كان ما به الاشتراك مستلزما لما به الامتياز لما كان ما به الامتياز موجبا للمايزة، وذلك محال، فلم يبق إلا القسم الرابع، وهو أن يكون ما به الممايزة مستلزما لما به المشاركة. وهذا يقتضي الجزم بتعليل الأشياء المتساوية بالعلل المختلفة.
That which constitutes similarity is different from that which constitutes distinction. If this is established, we say: Either there is no entailment between the two considerations at all, or each one entails the other, or that which constitutes commonality entails that which constitutes differentiation, or the converse. The first division is false, otherwise each of these two considerations would be separate from the other, and then no single reality would result from their combination, which is a contradiction. The second and third are also false, because if that which constitutes commonality entailed that which constitutes differentiation, then that which constitutes differentiation would not necessitate differentiation, which is impossible. So nothing remains except the fourth division, which is that that which constitutes differentiation entails that which constitutes commonality. And this necessitates the definite conclusion of explaining things that are equal by different causes.
Source pages p. 193

Commentary
This passage is a logical proof. Imam Razi begins by stating the premise that the aspect of similarity is different from the aspect of distinction. He then enumerates four possible relations between these two aspects: no entailment, mutual entailment, similarity entails distinction, or distinction entails similarity. He eliminates each possibility: no entailment would prevent a single reality from forming; mutual entailment or similarity entailing distinction would make the aspect of distinction ineffective. Only the fourth remains: the aspect of distinction entails the aspect of similarity. This implies that things sharing a common attribute must be explained by different underlying causes.

Key Terms
ما به التشابه
ما به الامتياز
ملازمة
علة
ما به المشاركة
ما به الممايزة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think this argument is about logical predication between concepts rather than about metaphysical causation.
Correction: Imam Razi is discussing actual aspects (al-iʿtibārāt) that constitute the reality of things, not merely logical categories. The entailment relations are causal or ontological, not merely logical.

Checkpoint
According to Imam Razi, which of the four possible relations between the aspect of similarity and the aspect of distinction is the only valid one?
A) No entailment
B) Mutual entailment
C) Similarity entails distinction
D) Distinction entails similarity

Key Takeaway
The aspect of distinction must entail the aspect of commonality, which implies that similar effects require different causes.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Take a moment to think about one specific quality that makes you distinct from those around you—perhaps a skill, a trial, or a blessing. Can you truly see this distinction as a pointer to a common Creator? How would your gratitude change if you saw every distinct aspect of your life as proof that you are not self-sufficient but entirely contingent on a single Origin?
?
This argument shows that no two things can be identical in every way without being the same reality—each has a unique cause. Now consider your most private struggles: do you ever feel alone in them, as if no one understands? But the lesson says that even your unique struggles share a common root with all human suffering. Does this realization drive you to seek the One who causes all things, or do you remain isolated in your distinctiveness?
?
If distinction entails commonality, then the very things that separate you from others—your opinions, your habits, your strengths—are signs that you share a universal human nature. How does this truth challenge your pride? When was the last time you thanked Allah not just for what you have, but for the very fact that you exist as a distinct person in a world of common creation?

The Regress Argument for Common Judgments
Imam Razi's regress argument against explaining common judgments by common attributes

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Imam Razi's regress argument that common judgments cannot be explained by common attributes but must terminate at particular essences.

Core Idea
Common judgments ultimately point to unique essences, not shared attributes.

Imam Razi presents a logical argument that if a common judgment (e.g., existence) required a common attribute, then that attribute itself would require another, leading to an infinite regress. The only way out is to trace explanations back to the particularities of essences. This argument is foundational in understanding causality in Islamic theology.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, paying attention to the conditional structure. Then read the translation to grasp the flow.
2
Identify the regress chain
Trace the chain: common judgment → requires common attribute → that attribute itself requires another common attribute, and so on. Notice the phrase 'فيذلم التسلسل'.
The regress is presented as a reductio ad absurdum.
3
Understand the conclusion
Since infinite regress is impossible, the assumption that common judgments require common attributes is false. The explanation must stop at the particularities of essences.
This leads to the conclusion that equal judgments are explained by different essences.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الرابع لو وجب تعليل الحكم المشترك بوصف مشترك لكان صحة كونه موصوفًا بذلك الوصف المشترك، معللة بوصف آخر مشترك فيه، والكلام فيه كما في الأول، فيلزم التسلسل، وهو محال، فعلمنا أنه لا بد من انتهاء تعليل الأحكام المشتركة إلى خصوصيات الماهيات وحينئذ يلزم تعليل الأحكام المتساوية بالماهيات المختلفة. لا يقال : الدليل على أن تعليل الأحكام المتساوية بالعلل المختلفة لا يجوز، وذلك لأن هذا الحكم لما كان معللا بهذه العلة لذاته، والحكم الثاني مساويا للحكم الأول في الماهية وللمتساويان في الماهية يجب استواؤهما في اللوازم، فيلزم أن يكون الحكم الثاني مستندا إلى ما يماثل تلك العلة فثبت أن الحكمين المتساويين يجب تعليلهما بعلتين متماثلتين. لأنا نقول : إن صح هذا الكلام لزم تعليل جميع الأحكام المتساوية بعلة واحدة بالشخص؛ لأن كون هذا الحكم معللا بهذه العلة المعينة أمر ثابت له لذاته، والحكم المتماثلة له مساو له في تمام الماهية، فيلزم في مثل ذلك الحكم أن يكون معللا بعين تلك العلة، وبالاتفاق هذا باطل، وكذا ما ذكرتموه. ثم نقول : لم لا يجوز أن يقال: إسناد هذا الحكم إلى هذه العلة ليس متولدا من ذات الحكم، بل العلة المخصوصة لذاتها توجب ذلك الحكم، ولما حصل ذلك الحكم بتلك العلة وجب إسناده إلى تلك العلة لا لأن ذاته يوجب الإسناد إليها، بل لأجل أن تكون العلة مستلزمة له، فوجب إسناده إليها، وهذا كلام دقيق لا بد من التأمل فيه.
Fourth: If it were necessary to explain a common judgment by a common attribute, then the correctness of its being described by that common attribute would itself be explained by another common attribute, and the same discussion applies as in the first, leading to an infinite regress, which is impossible. So we know that the explanation of common judgments must ultimately terminate at the particularities of essences, and then it follows that judgments that are equal are explained by different essences. It cannot be said: The proof that explaining equal judgments by different causes is impermissible is because this judgment, since it is explained by this cause for its essence, and the second judgment is equal to the first in essence, and things equal in essence must be equal in their consequents, so it would follow that the second judgment is based on something similar to that cause, thus establishing that equal judgments must be explained by two similar causes. We say: If this statement is correct, it would necessitate explaining all equal judgments by a single individual cause, because the fact that this judgment is explained by this specific cause is a fixed matter for its essence, and a judgment similar to it is equal to it in the entire essence, so for such a judgment it would follow that it is explained by that same cause, and by agreement this is false, and so is what you mentioned. Then we say: Why is it not permissible to say that the attribution of this judgment to this cause does not arise from the essence of the judgment, but rather the specific cause, due to its own essence, necessitates that judgment; and when that judgment is obtained by that cause, it is necessary to attribute it to that cause, not because its essence necessitates the attribution, but because the cause necessitates it, so it is necessary to attribute it to it. This is a subtle point that requires reflection.
Source pages p. 193

Commentary
This passage contains a fourth argument (الرابع) followed by an objection and response. The argument uses regress: if a common judgment required a common attribute, that attribute itself would require another common attribute, leading to an infinite regress, which is impossible. Therefore, explanation must ultimately resort to the particularities of essences, meaning that equal judgments are explained by different essences. An objection is raised: equal judgments must be explained by similar causes due to equality in essence. The author refutes this by showing that if that were true, all equal judgments would need the exact same cause, which is false. He then offers a subtle point: the attribution of a judgment to a cause is not due to the judgment's essence but due to the specific cause's essence necessitating the judgment. This requires careful reflection.

Key Terms
تعليل
حكم مشترك
وصف مشترك
تسلسل
ماهية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Common judgments require an infinite chain of common attributes.
Correction: The regress shows that such a chain is impossible, so explanation must terminate at particular essences.

Checkpoint
According to the regress argument, common judgments must ultimately be explained by what?
A) Common attributes
B) Particular essences
C) Infinite regress
D) Similar causes

Key Takeaway
Common judgments cannot be explained by common attributes; they must be traced to the unique particularities of essences.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You often explain things by pointing to shared traits—'I am kind because I have a good heart.' But if that 'good heart' itself needs explanation, where does it end? Are you resting your identity on attributes that are not ultimately grounded? What is the one unique essence that defines who you really are, beyond all shared descriptions?
?
If common judgments like existence cannot be explained by common attributes, then your own existence is not just another instance of a generic 'being.' It is a unique reality. Do you live as if your existence is a generic fact, or do you honor its unique particularity? What would change if you truly believed your essence is singular and irreplaceable?
?
The argument forces you to trace every explanation back to a stopping point. Where have you stopped explaining? Have you allowed an infinite regress of excuses, justifications, or dependencies in your relationship with Allah? What is the final particularity of your soul that only Allah knows, and are you willing to be defined by it?

Objection and Refutation: Equal Judgments and Similar Causes
Imam Razi's objection and refutation regarding equal judgments and similar causes

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the objection that equal judgments require similar causes and Imam Razi's refutation showing that would lead to a single cause for all equal judgments.

Core Idea
If equal judgments needed similar causes, they would need identical causes—which is absurd.

After establishing that equal judgments are explained by different essences, an objection is raised: since the judgment is attached to its cause by essence, equal judgments must have similar causes. Imam Razi refutes this by showing that the same logic would force all equal judgments to share the exact same cause, which is false. This lesson covers the objection and its rebuttal.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
Read the objector's argument: because the judgment is explained by its cause due to its essence, and equal judgments have equal essences, they must have similar consequents and thus similar causes.
2
Read the refutation
Imam Razi retorts: if that logic holds, then all equal judgments would need the exact same individual cause, which is false by agreement.
This is a reductio ad absurdum: the objection's premise leads to an unacceptable conclusion.
3
Reflect on the refutation
Understand why the objection fails: it overgeneralizes from one judgment to all equal judgments, ignoring that causes can be specific.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

?

The Subtle Point: Causation from the Cause's Essence
Imam Razi's subtle causation point: the cause's own essence necessitates the judgment

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Imam Razi's subtle point that the attribution of a judgment to a cause is due to the cause's own essence necessitating the judgment, not due to the judgment's essence.

Core Idea
A cause necessitates its effect by its own nature, not by the nature of the effect.

Imam Razi offers a refined explanation: the connection between a cause and its judgment is not derived from the judgment's essence but from the cause's essence. This allows equal judgments to have different causes. This subtle point resolves the earlier objection and is crucial for understanding causality in Islamic theology.

Guided Path
1
Read the alternative explanation
Read Imam Razi's proposal carefully. He distinguishes between attribution arising from the judgment's essence versus from the cause's essence.
2
Analyze the distinction
Note that he says the cause, due to its own essence, necessitates the judgment. The judgment is attributed to the cause because the cause entails it, not because the judgment requires that specific cause.
This is a key move to allow different causes for equal judgments.
3
Reflect on the subtlety
Imam Razi calls this a 'subtle point' that requires deep reflection. Consider how it solves the problem without falling into the objection's trap.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

The Eighth Objection: Conditions for an Effect
Razi objects that causation requires a receptive subject and absence of obstacles.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the presence of a cause alone does not guarantee an effect, and why the receptivity of the subject and absence of obstacles are necessary additional conditions.

Core Idea
A cause alone is never sufficient—receptivity and no obstacles are equally required.

Imam al-Razi presents an important objection that challenges a simplistic understanding of causality. Even if one grants that existence is the cause for the possibility of seeing God, the mere presence of a cause is not enough. We must also check whether the subject (God's essence) is receptive to the effect (being seen) and whether any obstacles prevent it. This lesson unpacks the logic of this objection.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
Read the Arabic and translation to understand the overall argument.
Notice the structure: a general principle followed by an example.
2
Identify the three conditions
The objection breaks down causation into three necessary elements: cause, receptive subject, and absence of obstacle. Find them in the text.
3
Analyze the example of life
Examine how the example of life in God illustrates that even with the cause (life), the effects (ignorance, desire, etc.) do not occur, proving the need for additional conditions.
This shows that either the subject is not receptive or there is an obstacle.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
السؤال الثامن : هب أن الوجود علة لصحة الرؤية ولكن كما يعتبر في حصول الحكم حصول العلة المؤثرة يعتبر أيضًا حصول المحل القابل، ويعتبر فيه عدم المانع، ألا ترى أن الحياة علة الجهل والشهوة والنفرة والألم واللذة ثم الحياة حاصلة في ذات الله. وصحة هذه الأشياء غير حاصلة، إما لأن تلك الذات المخصوصة غير قابلة لهذه الأحكام، أو لأنه قامت بتلك الذات صفات مانعة من تحقق هذه الأشياء. فثبت بهذا أن مع حصول العلة لا يتحقق الحكم إلا إذا ثبت أن المحل قابل. والمانع زائل، فلم قلتم إن خصوصية ذات الله تعالى قابلة لهذه الصحة ؟ ولم قلتم : إنه يوجد هناك ما يكون مانعا هذه الصحة ؟
The eighth question: Suppose that existence is the cause for the validity of seeing, but just as in the occurrence of a judgment the presence of the effective cause is considered, so also the presence of the receptive subject is considered, and the absence of an obstacle is considered. Do you not see that life is the cause of ignorance, desire, aversion, pain, and pleasure, yet life is present in the essence of God, but the validity of these things is not present, either because that particular essence is not receptive to these judgments, or because attributes exist in that essence that prevent the realization of these things. So it is established by this that with the presence of the cause, the judgment does not occur unless it is established that the subject is receptive and the obstacle is removed. So why do you say that the particularity of the essence of God Almighty is receptive to this validity? And why do you say that there does not exist something that is an obstacle to this validity?
Source pages p. 195

Commentary
This passage presents the eighth objection, which focuses on the conditions required for an effect. Even if existence is the cause for seeing, the objector argues that three conditions are necessary: (1) the presence of the effective cause, (2) the presence of a receptive subject, and (3) the absence of an obstacle. The objector illustrates this with the example of life: life is a cause for various states like ignorance, desire, etc., yet God has life but none of those states occur. This shows that even when the cause is present, the effect may not follow due to lack of receptivity or the presence of an obstacle. Therefore, to assert that God can be seen, one must prove that His essence is receptive to vision and that no obstacle exists. The objection challenges the proof to establish these two additional points.

Key Terms
علة مؤثرة (effective cause)
محل قابل (receptive subject)
مانع (obstacle)
حياة (life)
صفات مانعة (preventing attributes)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that if the cause is present, the effect must necessarily occur, without considering the subject's receptivity or obstacles.
Correction: This objection demonstrates that the presence of the cause alone is insufficient; the subject must be receptive and no obstacle may exist.

Checkpoint
What does the example of life demonstrate in the eighth objection?
A) Life is not a cause for any effect.
B) Life is a cause only in contingent beings.
C) Even when a cause exists, the effect may not follow if the subject is not receptive or an obstacle exists.
D) God does not have life.

Key Takeaway
The presence of a cause does not automatically produce its effect; the subject must be receptive and obstacles must be absent.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of a desire you have that you believe God should fulfill—perhaps a prayer for guidance or relief. Have you honestly examined whether your own heart is receptive to that effect? What specific obstacles of pride, distraction, or sin might be blocking the cause from working? This week, remove one such obstacle and note the difference.
?
When you face disappointment—when a good cause doesn't lead to the expected result—do you immediately blame God or question His attributes? How does this lesson's emphasis on receptivity and obstacles change your view of those disappointments? What does it demand of you to take responsibility for your own readiness?
?
The objection argues that God's essence may not be receptive to certain outcomes or that preventing attributes exist. How does this challenge your assumptions about how God 'should' act? Are you willing to accept that His ways are higher, and that your lack of understanding does not make Him unjust? What would it look like tomorrow to trust His wisdom even when the effect you seek is absent?

Eleventh Objection: Conditional Possibility of Seeing God
Razi objects that vision of God depends on a condition absent for us.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Imam Razi's objection that our inability to see God may be due to a missing condition rather than an intrinsic impossibility in God.

Core Idea
Seeing God may be possible in essence but conditionally impossible for us.

Imam Razi presents an objection to the Ash'ari claim that God can be seen. He concedes that God is intrinsically visible, but argues that our seeing Him may depend on a condition that we cannot fulfill. This shifts the discussion from whether vision is possible at all to whether it is possible for us.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic and its translation without analyzing deeply. Let the flow of the argument sink in.
Notice the conditional language: 'may be dependent' and 'impossible in our case'.
2
Identify the objection
Razi concedes God is visible in essence, but questions the condition for our seeing Him. Find the key phrase: 'but why is it not possible that our seeing Him is dependent upon a condition?'
3
Examine the analogy
Razi compares vision to creation: a body can be created in essence, but not by humans because we lack the condition (power). Understand how the impossibility is conditional, not intrinsic.
This analogy is central to the objection.
4
Apply to vision
Similarly, seeing God may be impossible for us not because God cannot be seen, but because a necessary condition (e.g., a specific ocular disposition) is absent in our case.
This preserves the Ash'ari position that God is visible in the afterlife, but objects to it being actual here.
5
Reflect on implications
Consider how this objection moves the debate from intrinsic possibility to conditional possibility. It challenges the necessity of vision in the afterlife.
This is a classic kalam move modifying the modality.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
السؤال الحادي عشر : أنه تعالى يصح أن يكون مرئيا في ذاته لكن لم لا يجوز أن تكون رؤيتنا له موقوفة على شرط يمتنع ثبوته في حقنا، فلا جرم يمتنع المشروط لامتناع ذلك الشرط، وهذا كما أن الجسم في نفسه يصح أن يكون مخلوقا، لكنه يمتنع أن يكون مخلوقا لنا لأن صحة خلق الجسم مشروطة بشرط يمتنع ثبوته في حقنا، ففاتت هذه الصحة في حقنا، لا لأن تلك الصحة في نفسها فائتة، بل لأن شرط تلك الصحة فائت في حقنا، وكذا القول في مسألتنا.
The eleventh question: That He, exalted, is valid in His essence to be seen, but why is it not possible that our seeing Him is dependent upon a condition whose existence is impossible in our case? Consequently, the conditioned is impossible due to the impossibility of that condition. This is like the case that a body in itself is valid to be created, but it is impossible that it be created by us, because the validity of creating a body is conditioned by a condition whose existence is impossible in our case. So this validity is absent in our case, not because that validity is absent in itself, but because the condition of that validity is absent in our case. And similarly is the statement in our issue.
Source pages p. 196

Commentary
This passage presents the eleventh objection, which introduces the concept of conditional possibility. The objector concedes that God may be intrinsically visible, but argues that our seeing Him could depend on a condition that we cannot fulfill. For example, a body is in itself possible to be created, but it cannot be created by human beings because we lack the necessary power. The impossibility of the conditioned (our creating) is due to the absence of the condition, not because the act of creation is impossible in itself. Similarly, the impossibility of our seeing God may be due to the absence of a condition (like a specific ocular disposition or divine will) rather than any intrinsic impossibility in God being seen. This objection shifts the focus from the cause to the conditions required for the effect to occur.

Key Terms
مرئي في ذاته (visible in essence)
شرط (condition)
مشروط (conditioned)
امتناع (impossibility)
موقوفة (dependent)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If God is intrinsically visible, then He must be seen by everyone.
Correction: Razi shows that even if something is possible in itself, its actual occurrence may depend on conditions that are absent for some, so vision of God may be possible in essence but not for us due to missing conditions.

Checkpoint
What does the example of creation illustrate in the eleventh objection?
A) That bodies cannot be created at all.
B) That something possible in itself may be impossible for us due to missing conditions.
C) That creation is impossible for God.
D) That vision of God is intrinsically impossible.

Key Takeaway
The impossibility of seeing God may be due to a missing condition, not an intrinsic impossibility in God.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
What specific condition in your own life—a habit, a distraction, or a sin—might be preventing you from experiencing a deeper closeness to God? What would it cost you to remove that condition today?
?
If seeing God is possible in essence but impossible for you due to a missing condition, what does that say about your current spiritual state? Are you actively seeking that condition, or have you accepted the impossibility as permanent?
?
The analogy compares our inability to see God to our inability to create a body. What are you trying to achieve in your spiritual life by your own power, when only God can provide the necessary condition? Will you stop striving in your own strength and instead ask Him to grant the condition?

Twelfth Objection: The Parity Argument against Visibility Proof
Razi uses a parity argument: if existence were cause for visibility, it would also cause creation, an absurdity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Razi's twelfth objection uses a parity argument to show that if existence were the cause for visibility, it would absurdly imply God is created.

Core Idea
Parity shows that the same logic undermines the proof, not the attribute.

Razi now presents the twelfth objection, which turns the proponent's own reasoning against him. Using a parallel case—the validity of being created—the objector shows that the same method of finding a shared cause leads to an absurd conclusion. This forces the proponent's proof to fail by its own standards.

Guided Path
1
Understand the objection's premise
The objector starts by noting that God can create both substances and accidents, so 'being created' is a judgment common to both.
This parallels the original proof where visibility was common to substances and accidents.
2
Identify the search for a common cause
The objector argues that 'being created' must have a cause shared by both categories—only temporal origination (huduth) or existence (wujud) are possible.
Temporal origination was already rejected as a cause in the original proof.
3
Follow the reductio ad absurdum
Since huduth is invalid, existence must be the cause. But then God exists, so He would be created—an absurdity.
The conclusion is false, so the reasoning that led to it must be flawed.
4
Apply the refutation to the original proof
The same logic was used to claim existence is the cause for visibility. If that reasoning leads to absurdity here, it is also invalid there.
Thus the original proof for visibility is refuted by parity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
السؤال الثاني عشر : ما ذكرتم من الدلائل معارض بما أن الله تعالى قادر على خلق الجواهر وعلى خلق الأعراض فصحة المخلوقية حكم مشترك فيه بين الجواهر والأعراض، فلا بد من تعليل هذه الصحة بأمر مشترك بين القسمين، ولا مشترك إلا الحدوث أو الوجود، والحدوث باطل لما ذكرتم فثبت أن علة صحة المخلوقية هي الوجود، والله تعالى موجود فوجب صحة كونه تعالى مخلوقا، ولما بطل هذا الكلام فكذا ما ذكرتم.
The twelfth question: What you have mentioned of proofs is opposed by the fact that God is able to create substances and accidents. So the validity of being created is a judgment shared between substances and accidents. So it is necessary to explain this validity by a matter shared between the two categories. And there is no shared matter except temporality or existence. And temporality is invalid according to what you mentioned. So it is established that the cause of the validity of being created is existence. And God exists, so the validity of His being created necessarily follows. And when this statement is invalid, so is what you mentioned.
Source pages p. 196

Commentary
This passage presents the twelfth objection, which is a parity argument. The objector notes that God is able to create both substances and accidents, so being created is a common attribute among them. Following the same method of the original proof, one must find a shared attribute that is the cause of this 'validity of being created.' The only possible shared attributes are temporal origination (huduth) and existence. But temporal origination is invalid as a cause (as the proponent previously argued). Therefore existence must be the cause. Since God exists, it would follow that God is created — an absurd conclusion. Since this conclusion is false, the original proof that existence is the cause for visibility is also false, as it relies on the same reasoning.

Key Terms
مخلوقية (being created)
جواهر (substances)
أعراض (accidents)
حدوث (temporal origination)
معارض (opposed/refuted by)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The objection disproves God's power or existence.
Correction: The objection only shows the logical flaw in the proof for visibility; it does not deny God's attributes or power.

Checkpoint
What absurd conclusion does the twelfth objection draw from the same reasoning?
A) That God cannot create substances.
B) That God would be created if existence is the cause of being created.
C) That existence is not shared.
D) That the proof is valid.

Key Takeaway
The twelfth objection demonstrates that the same reasoning used to prove visibility would, if applied consistently, imply that God is created — a clear absurdity that refutes the proof.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever defended a belief using a standard of proof that, if applied consistently, would also justify a falsehood you reject? Will you commit to examining your arguments for hidden contradictions today?
?
The objector here forces the proponent to follow his own logic to an absurd conclusion. When was the last time you allowed someone to challenge your reasoning to its logical extreme without becoming defensive? What did you discover about the strength of your position?
?
If your cherished belief about God or religion relies on a method of reasoning that can be turned against itself, are you willing to abandon that method and seek a more solid foundation? This lesson shows that even a seemingly strong proof can collapse under scrutiny. What is one argument you rely on that you have not rigorously tested? Will you test it this week?
