Proof that Nothing Exists Except by God’s Power
Razi introduces the twenty-third issue and presents the first two proofs.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Razi's two rational proofs that no created being can independently bring about existence from non-existence; only God's power is efficacious.

Core Idea
Only God's power can create existence from nothing.

Razi addresses a key theological dispute: whether any being other than God can cause something to exist. He provides two concise proofs based on the nature of possibility (imkān) and the requirement for a specific effecter. These arguments aim to establish that divine power alone is the source of all creation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without overanalyzing. Notice the two proofs introduced by 'الأول' and 'الثاني'. Then read the translation to grasp the overall argument.
The passage is dense; take your time.
2
Understand the first proof
Focus on how Razi uses the concept of possibility (imkān) as a shared cause, and how God's essence relates equally to all possibles, leading to the conclusion that no other effecter can coexist with God's power.
Pay attention to the three invalid scenarios when two effecters are supposed.
3
Understand the second proof
Notice that possibility necessitates a specific effecter. Reason through why the effecter cannot be non-specific and cannot be a possible being itself, avoiding circularity.
This proof shows the effecter must be the Necessary Being.
4
Reflect on the conclusion
Combine both proofs: God's power covers all possibles, and any effecter must be God. Thus, nothing comes into existence except by His power.
This reinforces tawheed and refutes the idea of independent secondary causes in creation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المسألة الثالثة والعشرون في أنه لا يخرج شيء من عدم إلى الوجود إلا بقدرة الله تعالى اعلم أن أكثر أرباب الملل يخالفوننا في هذه المسألة ولو أطنبنا في شرح المذاهب وإيطالها ، لطال الكتاب إلا أنا ههنا نكتفي بالاختصار. فنقول : الذي يدل على صحة قولنا وجوه : الأول : إن علة صحة المقدورية هي الإمكان والإمكان حكم مشترك فيه بين كل الممكنات وإذا كانت العلة مشتركة فيها، كان الحكم كذلك فإذن كل حكم الممكنات مشتركة في كونها بحيث يصح أن تكون مقدورة لله تعالى والمقتضي لكونه قادرا على المقدور هو ذاته ونسبة الذات إلى الكل على السوية. فلما اقتضت الذات كونه تعالى قادرا على البعض، وجب أن تقتضي كونه قادرا على الكل. فثبت أنه تعالى قادر على كل الممكنات، فلو فرضنا شيئا آخر يصلح لأن يكون مؤثرا في الوجود، فعند فرض اجتماع هذين المؤثرين إما أن يقع الأثر بهما، أو لا يقع بواحد منها، أو يقع بأحدهما دون الثاني والأقسام الثلاثة باطلة، لما مرت الإشارة إليه في مسألة التوحيد ومسألة خلق الأفعال فوجب القطع بأنه لا شيء سوى قدرة الله تعالى يصلح للتأثير والتكوين. الثاني: أن صفة الإمكان صفة واحدة في الممكنات، وإنها محوجة إلى المؤثر. فإما أن يقال : الإمكان يحوج إلى مؤثر معين أو يحوج إلى مؤثر غير معين . والثاني محال . لأن ما لا يكون معينًا في نفسه لا يكون موجودًا وما لا يكون موجودًا استحال احتياج غيره إليه في الوجود . فإذن الإمكان يحوج إلى شيء معين. وذلك المعين إما أن يكون من الممكنات وإما أن لا يكون . لا جائز أن يكون من الممكنات وإلا لكان إمكان ذلك الشيء يحوجه إلى نفسه وحينئذ يكون موجدا لنفسه.
The twenty-third issue: That nothing comes out from non-existence into existence except by the power of God Almighty. Know that most of the people of [various] religions oppose us in this issue, and if we were to expand in explaining the schools and refuting them, the book would become lengthy, but here we suffice with brevity. So we say: That which indicates the correctness of our statement are [several] aspects. The first: Indeed the cause of the validity of being powerful over something is possibility (al-imkan), and possibility is a ruling shared among all possible things. And when the cause is shared among them, the ruling is likewise. Thus all possible things are shared in being such that they are valid to be powerful over for God Almighty. And that which necessitates that He is powerful over the powerful-over (al-maqdur) is His essence, and the relation of the essence to all is equal. So when the essence necessitated that He, exalted, is powerful over some, it must necessitate that He is powerful over all. So it is established that He, exalted, is powerful over all possible things. So if we suppose another thing that is suitable to be an effecter in existence, then upon the supposition of the conjunction of these two effecters, either the effect occurs by both, or by neither, or by one without the other. And the three divisions are invalid, as was previously indicated in the issue of monotheism and the issue of the creation of acts. So it is necessary to conclude that there is nothing other than the power of God Almighty that is suitable for effecting and bringing into existence. The second: Indeed the attribute of possibility is one attribute in possible things, and it necessitates an effecter. So either it is said that possibility necessitates a specific effecter, or it necessitates a non-specific effecter. The second is impossible, because that which is not specific in itself does not exist, and that which does not exist cannot be needed by another for existence. So possibility necessitates something specific. And that specific either is from among possible things or is not. It cannot be from among possible things, because then the possibility of that thing would necessitate itself, and in that case it would be a creator of itself.
Source pages p. 234

Commentary
This passage introduces the twenty-third issue and presents two proofs that nothing comes into existence except by God's power. Razi begins by noting that most religious groups oppose this view, but he will be brief. He then presents the first proof: The cause (علة) of a thing being a valid object of power (مقدورية) is possibility (الإمكان). Possibility is a shared attribute among all possible things. Since the cause is shared, the ruling (that something can be an object of divine power) is also shared. The essence of God is the ground for His power, and His essence relates equally to all possibles. So if His essence makes Him powerful over some possibles, it must make Him powerful over all. Hence God is powerful over all possibles. Now if we suppose another being that could be an effecter, then if both God and that being were to act together on the same effect, either the effect occurs by both, or by neither, or by one alone. All three possibilities are invalid for reasons already given in the discussions of tawheed and creation of acts. Therefore, nothing other than God's power is fit for effecting and originating. The second proof: Possibility (الإمكان) is a single attribute in all possible things, and it necessitates an effecter (يحتاج إلى مؤثر). The effecter must be either specific or non-specific. A non-specific effecter is impossible because what is not specific does not exist, and a non-existent thing cannot be needed. So the effecter must be specific. That specific effecter is either itself a possible being or not. It cannot be a possible being, because then the possibility of that being would necessitate itself (circular), and it would be a self-creator. Thus the effecter must be something other than possible things, i.e., the necessary being, God.

Key Terms
الإمكان
المقدورية
المقتضي
المؤثر
التكوين

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think Razi denies all forms of causality, including secondary causality.
Correction: Razi only denies that any created being can independently bring about existence from non-existence; he does not deny other forms of causality, such as the causal relationships among created things that are themselves dependent on God.

Checkpoint
In the first proof, why does Razi argue that no other effecter besides God can bring about existence?
A) Because possibility is shared, so God's power must extend to all possibles, leaving no room for another.
B) Because if another effecter existed, the effect would have to be produced by both, which is impossible.
C) Both A and B are part of the proof.
D) Because the other effecter would also be a possible being, leading to circularity.

Key Takeaway
Razi argues that since possibility is shared and the effecter must be specific and not a possible being, only God can bring things into existence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you truly rely on God's power alone for every outcome in your life, or do you unconsciously attribute power to your efforts, health, or other means? If God were to withdraw His power for a single moment, what would become of your plans? Sit in silence and consider one area where you fear losing control—what would it mean to entrust that entirely to God?
?
The passage argues that possibility itself demands a specific effecter. Where in your life do you treat possibilities as if they can actualize themselves without God's direct intervention? Name one ambition you are currently pursuing and ask yourself: am I acting as if my success depends on my own ability or on God's will?
?
Razi shows that if another effecter existed, it would lead to logical contradictions. Reflect on a time you doubted God's exclusive power—perhaps when you saw injustice or difficulty. How does this proof challenge that doubt? Will you commit tonight to affirming before sleep: 'There is no creator but God, and nothing happens except by His power'?

Third Proof: Possibles Are Recipients, Not Agents
Razi's third proof from philosophical principles

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Imam Razi's third proof that possible beings cannot be agents and that only the Necessary Being is a true agent.

Core Idea
Possible beings can only receive, not give existence.

Imam Razi presents a tightly reasoned argument drawn from philosophical principles. He contends that if a possible being could cause something else to exist, it would have to be both a receiver of existence (from another) and a giver of existence simultaneously—a contradiction. This lesson walks through that argument step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the proof in Arabic and English
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice the key terms: ممكن, قابل, فاعل, واجب لذاته.
The proof is concise; pay attention to the logical connector 'فلو' (if then).
2
Identify the core premise
The argument starts with the premise: every possible being is receptive to both existence and non-existence. Why does this matter?
This receptivity means it cannot give what it needs to receive itself.
3
Follow the conditional reasoning
Notice the conditional: if a possible were an agent, it would be both receptive and active. Ask why this is impossible.
The impossibility is logical—one thing cannot have two contradictory properties.
4
Understand the conclusion
The proof concludes that possibles have only receptivity, while the Necessary has only agency. This establishes a clear division.
No possible can be a true cause; only Allah is the true agent.
5
Test yourself with the checkpoint question
Answer the multiple-choice question to check your understanding of the proof's logic.
The answer is based directly on the passage.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الثالثة : هي مبنية على أصول الحكماء - هي أن كل ما كان ممكنا لذاته، فهو قابل للوجود والعدم، فلو كان شيء من الممكنات مؤثرا في وجود شيء آخر، لزم كون الشيء الواحد قابلا وفاعلا معًا. وهو محال فإذن الممكن له خاصية القبول، وليس له صلاحية التأثير، كما أن الواجب لذاته له خاصية التأثير، وليس له صلاحية القبول.
The third proof: It is based on the principles of the philosophers – that everything that is possible in itself is receptive to existence and non-existence. So if something among possibles were an agent (muʾaththir) in the existence of something else, then one thing would be both receptive and agent simultaneously – that is impossible. Thus the possible has the property of receptivity, and does not have the fitness for agency, just as the necessary in itself has the property of agency and does not have the fitness for receptivity.
Source pages p. 234

Commentary
This passage contains the third proof, which is based on the philosophical principles accepted by both parties. Imam al-Razi argues that every possible being, by definition, is equally receptive to existence and non-existence. If a possible being were to act as an agent causing the existence of another thing, then that same possible being would have to be both a receiver (of existence from another) and a giver of existence simultaneously. This is a contradiction because an agent brings about existence, while a possible being itself stands in need of an agent to receive existence. Therefore, no possible being can be an agent. Hence only the necessary being, who is not receptive, can be the true agent. This argument effectively divides reality into two categories: the Necessary, which is pure agency, and the possible, which is pure receptivity.

Key Terms
ممكن
قابل
فاعل
واجب لذاته
قبول

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that this proof denies any secondary causality in the world, such as fire burning or a knife cutting.
Correction: The proof only denies intrinsic agency (ability to cause existence independently) to possible beings. It does not deny the apparent causal chains that are sustained by Allah. Possible beings can be instruments but not true agents.

Checkpoint
According to the third proof, why can a possible being not be an agent?
A) Because it is not powerful enough
B) Because it would require receiving existence from another while itself giving existence
C) Because only necessary beings can act
D) Because agency requires existence

Key Takeaway
The third proof shows that possible beings are purely receptive, leaving true agency exclusively to the Necessary Being (Allah).

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?
Consider your most cherished possession or accomplishment. In light of this proof, is there any room for boasting? Write a prayer of acknowledgment that you are merely a receiver, while Allah is the sole Giver of existence and goodness.

Learning Objective

A contradictory is simply the negation of a claim.

This lesson examines the opening line of Razi's response to the second argument. Though brief, this fragment establishes a critical logical step: clarifying what it means to negate a statement. Understanding this contradictory sets the stage for the full argument that follows on the next page.

3

The Third Doubt: Cause and Effect Compatibility
Imam al-Razi presents the third doubt about compatibility and its answer.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Imam al-Razi's response to the third objection about causality, specifically his critique of the notion of 'compatibility' between cause and effect.

Core Idea
Compatibility is undefined; if it means similarity, cause and effect cannot be alike.

In this passage, Imam al-Razi addresses the third doubt regarding causality. The objector claims that a cause must be compatible with its effect. Al-Razi challenges the meaning of 'compatibility' and shows that if it implies similarity, it leads to a contradiction. This lesson unpacks the objection and its refutation.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Read the objector's argument that cause and effect must be compatible, and that one thing cannot be compatible with two different effects without contradiction.
Notice the structure: the objector sets up a conditional argument.
2
Examine al-Razi's response
Al-Razi begins by stating that 'compatible' is not clearly defined. Then he considers the possible interpretation of similarity (mumathila).
Pay attention to how he refutes the similarity interpretation.
3
Understand the consequence
If compatibility means similarity, then cause and effect would be alike, but that would make the effect a cause and vice versa, which is impossible. Ask: what if the objector meant something else by compatibility?
Al-Razi leaves the burden of clarification on the objector.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الشبهة الثالثة : العلة لا بد أن تكون ملائمة للمعلول، والشيء الواحد بالاعتبار الواحد، لو أوجب الشيئين ،المختلفين لزم أن يكون الشيء الواحد بالاعتبار الواحد ملائما لشيئين مختلفين والملائم للشيئين المختلفة مختلف فيلزم أن يكون الشيء الواحد بالاعتبار الواحد، مخالفا لنفسه محال. وهو والجواب عن الشبهة الثالثة : أن الملائم غير ملخص المعنى . فإن أردتم به كون العلة مماثلة للمعلوم، فهذا خطأ . لأن المعلوم لذاته محتاج إلى العلة، والعلة لذاتها مؤثرة في المعلول، فلو كانت العلة والمعلول مثلين لزم كون العلة معلولا والمعلول علة وهو محال . وإن كان المراد من الملائمة شيئا آخر فلا بد من بيانه.
The third doubt: The cause must be compatible (mula'im) with the effect, and one thing in one respect, if it necessitates two different things, would entail that one thing in one respect is compatible with two different things, and that which is compatible with two different things is different, so it would entail that one thing in one respect is contrary to itself, which is impossible. It is... And the answer to the third doubt: That 'compatible' (al-mula'im) is not clearly defined. If you mean by it the cause being similar (mumathil) to the effect, then this is wrong, because the effect is in itself in need of the cause, and the cause in itself has influence on the effect. So if the cause and effect were alike, it would entail the cause being an effect and the effect being a cause, which is impossible. And if by compatibility something else is meant, then it must be explained.
Source pages p. 236

Commentary
This passage presents the third objection and its refutation. The objector argues that a cause must be compatible with its effect. If one thing in one respect gives rise to two different effects, it would have to be compatible with both, which would require it to be different from itself (since compatibility with different things would require different natures). That is impossible. Al-Razi's answer challenges the undefined term 'compatible' (al-mula'im). If 'compatible' means 'similar' (mumathil), then that is false because the cause and effect are ontologically distinct: the effect depends on the cause, and the cause has efficacy over the effect. If they were similar, each would be both cause and effect, which is contradictory. If something else is meant, the objector must clarify. Thus the objection fails due to equivocation.

Key Terms
الملائمة
المماثلة
العلة
المعلول

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that cause and effect must be similar (compatible) in essence.
Correction: Al-Razi shows that similarity is impossible because the effect depends on the cause, and the cause has efficacy over the effect; they cannot be alike without circularity.

Checkpoint
What does al-Razi say about the term 'compatibility' (al-mula'im) in the answer?
A) It clearly means similarity between cause and effect
B) It is not clearly defined and if it means similarity it is false
C) It means the cause and effect are identical
D) It means the cause and effect are opposites

Key Takeaway
Al-Razi refutes the third doubt by exposing the ambiguity of 'compatibility' and showing that similarity between cause and effect is impossible.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?
The objector insisted on compatibility; al-Razi exposed it as ambiguous. Have you ever used words like 'fair' or 'makes sense' about God's actions without really defining what they mean? How can you train yourself to pause and question whether your own vocabulary about God is truly grounded in revelation and reason? Commit to memorizing one Qur'anic verse that defines God's names and attributes, to guard against ambiguous terms.
?
This passage shows that cause and effect cannot be similar because the effect depends entirely on the cause. In your own life, do you sometimes act as if your achievements are independent of God, as if you and God are 'partners' in causation? How does the impossibility of similarity between cause and effect call you to a deeper reliance on God alone for every outcome? The Fourth Doubt: Different Effects vs. Absence of Effects
Al-Razi presents the fourth doubt and its answer, distinguishing ikhtilaf and takhaluf.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the distinction between 'difference of effects' (ikhtilaf al-athar) and 'absence of effects' (takhaluf al-athar) in causal inference according to Imam al-Razi.

Core Idea
The real indicator of different natures is absence, not mere difference, of effects.

This passage presents the fourth objection against God's unity, based on observable causal differences. The objector argues that different effects (like heating from fire and cooling from water) indicate different causes, which would challenge monotheism. Imam al-Razi's answer refines the logic, showing that it is the absence of one effect from the other cause—not mere difference—that truly indicates distinct natures.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic source and its literal translation carefully. Note the two key terms: ikhtilaf (difference) and takhaluf (absence).
The passage is short but dense; read it twice.
2
Identify the objector's reasoning
The objector uses fire and water as examples. He argues: different effects → different natures → multiple causes. Understand this syllogism.
The objection attacks tawhid by suggesting observed diversity requires multiple creators.
3
Grasp al-Razi's distinction
Al-Razi says the real proof of different natures is not that fire heats and water cools, but that water is found without heating and fire without cooling. That is takhaluf al-athar (absence of effects).
Absence means one cause lacks the effect of the other, showing their natures are distinct.
4
Reflect on the implication
The objection fails because mere difference in effects (ikhtilaf) does not necessitate different causes; it is the absence (takhaluf) that does. This preserves the possibility of a single cause producing diverse effects.
This answer protects the principle of divine unity while explaining observed diversity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الشبهة الرابعة لما شاهدنا أن تأثير النار هو التسخين، وتأثير الماء هو التبريد، استدللنا بهذين الأثرين على أن طبيعة الماء مخالفة لطبيعة النار، وإذا كان اختلاف الأثر دالا على اختلاف المؤثر، فبأن يدل على تعدد المؤثر أولى. والجواب عن الشبهة الرابعة أن الذي به عرفنا أن طبيعة الماء تخالف طبيعة النار، ليس هو مجرد اختلاف الأثرين بل لما رأينا الماء حاصلا بدون التسخين، والنار حاصلة بدون التبريد علمنا بهذا اختلاف طبيعتيهما . فالحاصل : أن المعرف لاختلاف الطبيعتين هو تخلف الآثار. لا اختلاف الآثار.
The fourth doubt: Since we observe that the effect of fire is heating and the effect of water is cooling, we infer from these two effects that the nature of water is different from the nature of fire. And if difference of effect indicates difference of cause, then it indicates multiplicity of cause even more so. And the answer to the fourth doubt: That by which we know that the nature of water differs from the nature of fire is not merely the difference of the two effects. Rather, when we saw water occurring without heating, and fire occurring without cooling, we knew thereby the difference of their two natures. So the conclusion: That which identifies the difference of the two natures is the absence (takhaluf) of effects, not the difference (ikhtilaf) of effects.
Source pages p. 236

Commentary
This passage presents the fourth doubt and its refutation. The objector uses the example of fire and water: fire heats, water cools. The difference in effects leads us to infer a difference in their natures. Similarly, if two different effects proceed from one cause, that would indicate the cause has multiple natures, thus contradicting the principle of unity. Al-Razi's answer clarifies the epistemological basis: The difference in effects alone does not necessarily indicate a difference in natures. For example, fire could potentially produce cooling if it had that potency unknown to us. The real indicator is the absence (takhaluf) of one effect when the other is present. We know water is not fire because water does not heat; and fire is not water because fire does not cool. So it is the fact that water is found without heating (i.e., the absence of heating) that tells us its nature is different from fire's. So the mere difference in effects is not the proof; it is the absence of one effect from the other cause. Thus the objection fails.

Key Terms
الأثر
المؤثر
الطبيعة
تخلف الآثار
اختلاف الآثار

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Observing that fire heats and water cools necessarily proves that fire and water have different natures and different causes.
Correction: Al-Razi shows that the mere difference of effects does not prove different natures; it is the absence of each effect from the other substance (water without heating, fire without cooling) that actually indicates different natures.

Checkpoint
According to al-Razi, what truly indicates that the natures of water and fire are different?
A) The fact that fire heats and water cools
B) The fact that water is found without heating and fire is found without cooling
C) The fact that water and fire are opposites
D) The fact that water extinguishes fire

Key Takeaway
It is the absence of one effect from the other cause, not mere difference in effects, that reveals distinct natures—a crucial distinction for defending divine unity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever assumed that because two things appear different, they must come from different sources? How does al-Razi's distinction between ikhtilaf and takhaluf challenge your natural assumptions about causality? Commit to re-examining one assumption you hold about the causes behind events in your life.
?
When you see a variety of effects in the world—good and bad, pleasurable and painful—do you tend to attribute them to different causes, perhaps even forgetting the unity of the Creator? How can the concept of takhaluf help you see all events as coming from one source? What would change if you truly internalized that?
?
Al-Razi shows that absence of effects, not difference, reveals distinct natures. In your own spiritual life, what 'effects' are you lacking that you should seek? What is absent that points to a needed change in your nature? Write down one concrete step to address that absence.

Al-Razi's Refutation of Astrologers
Al-Razi refutes astrologers via symmetry argument leading to compositeness.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Al-Razi refutes astrologers by showing that celestial bodies are subject to composition and dissolution, implying their contingency.

Core Idea
Simplicity implies symmetry, which implies compositeness—celestial bodies cannot be managers.

This passage presents Al-Razi's logical refutation of astrologers (munajjimun) who claim that the stars and planets govern the sublunar world. By analyzing the nature of celestial bodies, Al-Razi demonstrates that they are not eternal or self-sufficient, but rather contingent and composite. Understanding this argument strengthens the Islamic doctrine of Tawhid and exclusive divine causation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the literal translation. Let the rhythm of the argument come through before interpreting.
The text is slightly fragmented due to OCR, but the logical flow is clear.
2
Identify the logical steps
Notice the dilemma: celestial bodies are either simple or composite. If simple, they have symmetric sides, allowing interchange of right and left. Anything with such symmetry is subject to composition and dissolution.
This leads to the conclusion that celestial bodies are subject to change.
3
Reflect on the implications
Why does this refute astrology? If celestial bodies are changeable and composite, they cannot be eternal managers of the world. They are created and dependent on Allah.
The argument also undermines the philosophers' notion of eternal heavens.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفرقة الثالثة من المخالفين في هذه المسألة: المنجمون الذين يقولون : المدبر في هذا العالم السفلي هو الأفلاك والكواكب والذي يدل على بطلان قولهم : إن الأفلاك إما أن تكون بسائط أو مركبة من أجزاء كل واحد منها بسيط في نفسه . بسيط فإنه يحصل له جانبان بحيث يكون كل واحد منهما مساويا للآخر - بكل ما كان كذلك . - فما كان ممسوس يمينه صح أن ينقلب ممسوس يساره، وبالعكس. كل ما كان كذلك، فإن التركيب والانحلال جائزان عليه . فإذن التركيب والانحلال جائزان على أجرام الفلك والكواكب. فيلزم من هذه النكتة فساد أصول الفلاسفة، أصحاب
The third group of opponents in this issue: the astrologers (al-munajjimun) who say: The manager in this lower world is the celestial spheres and planets. And what indicates the falsity of their statement: That the celestial spheres are either simple (basait) or composed of parts each of which is simple in itself. If simple, then it has two sides such that each of them is equal to the other - in everything that is like that. So whatever has its right side touched, it is valid for it to be turned to its left side, and vice versa. Everything that is like that, then composition and dissolution are possible for it. So therefore composition and dissolution are possible for the celestial bodies and planets. So from this point follows the corruption of the principles of the philosophers, the practitioners...
Source pages p. 237

Commentary
This passage refutes the astrologers (munajjimun) who hold that the celestial spheres and planets govern the sublunar world. Al-Razi argues that the celestial spheres are either simple (basa'it) or composed of simple parts. If they are simple, they have symmetrical sides — for example, a simple sphere has no inherent orientation, so its right and left sides are interchangeable. Anything with such symmetry is subject to composition and dissolution (tarkib wa inhilal), meaning it is composite and changeable. Therefore, the celestial bodies are not eternal or immutable as the astrologers claim; they are contingent and created, thus cannot be the ultimate managers of the world. The argument leads to the corruption of the foundational principles of the philosophers (falasifa) who believe in the eternity of the heavens. The passage ends abruptly with 'أصحاب' — probably 'practitioners of astrology'.

Key Terms
المنجمون
الأفلاك
الكواكب
البسائط
التركيب
الانحلال

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think the celestial spheres are simple and unchanging, and thus eternal.
Correction: Al-Razi shows that simplicity leads to symmetry, and symmetry implies compositeness and changeability. Thus, celestial bodies are not eternal or self-sufficient.

Checkpoint

B) They are subject to composition and dissolution
C) They have no physical properties
D) They are made of fire

Key Takeaway
Al-Razi refutes astrologers by proving that celestial bodies are subject to change and composition, thus cannot be the managers of the world.

This argument shows that assumptions (like 'simple = unchanging') can crumble under scrutiny. What is one belief you hold that you have never questioned? Are you willing to subject it to the same rigorous logical test, even if it upsets your certainty?

Answer to the Third Doubt: The Meaning of Compatibility
Razi responds to the third objection about compatibility between cause and effect.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Razi's refutation of the claim that cause and effect must be compatible in the sense of similarity, and why such similarity would lead to logical circularity.

Core Idea
Cause and effect cannot be similar; if they were, each would need to be both cause and effect.

Razi continues his defense of causality by addressing a third objection: that a cause must be 'compatible' with its effect. But what does 'compatible' mean? If it means similarity, Razi shows that this leads to an impossible loop. This passage clarifies that the relationship between cause and effect is inherently asymmetrical.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice how Razi first critiques the ambiguity of the term 'compatible'.
2
Identify the two interpretations
Razi considers two possible meanings of 'compatible': similarity or something else. Focus on his refutation of the similarity interpretation.
3
Understand the logical refutation
Razi argues that if cause and effect were similar, each would need to be both cause and effect, which is impossible. See how this asymmetry is crucial.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والجواب عن الشبهة الثالثة : أن الملائم غير ملخص المعنى . فإن أردتم به كون العلة مماثلة للمعلوم، فهذا خطأ . لأن المعلوم لذاته محتاج إلى العلة، والعلة لذاتها مؤثرة في المعلول، فلو كانت العلة والمعلول مثلين لزم كون العلة معلول والمعلول علة وهو محال . وإن كان المراد من الملائمة شيئا آخر فلا بد من بيانه.
And the answer to the third doubt: that 'compatible' (al-mula'im) is not clear in meaning. For if you intend by it the cause being similar to the effect, this is false. Because the effect in its essence needs the cause, and the cause in its essence acts upon the effect, so if the cause and effect were two similars, it would necessitate the cause being an effect and the effect being a cause, which is impossible. And if by compatibility something else is meant, then it must be explained.
Source pages p. 237

Commentary
This passage is a response to a doubt about the concept of compatibility (al-mula'im) in causality. Razi first points out that the term 'compatible' is ambiguous. He then considers two possibilities: if the objector means that the cause must be similar to the effect, then he refutes that by noting the asymmetry: the effect depends on the cause while the cause acts on the effect; if they were similar, each would have to be both cause and effect, leading to circularity. If they mean something else, they must clarify. The argument is straightforward: similarity is not a necessary condition for causality, and if pushed, it leads to absurdity.

Key Terms
الملائم
العلة
المعلول
المماثلة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Cause and effect must be similar in nature to be compatible.
Correction: Razi shows that if they were similar, they would each need to be both cause and effect, which is logically impossible. The relationship is asymmetrical.

Checkpoint

B) It is not clearly defined, and if it means similarity, it is impossible.
C) It means the cause and effect are identical.
D) It is a term for divine attributes.

Key Takeaway
Razi refutes the notion that cause and effect must be similar, as similarity would lead to logical circularity.

Distinguishing Natures by Effects vs. Absence of Effects
Razi explains that different natures are known by the absence of mutual effects, not by differing effects alone.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Razi argues that the absence of mutual effects, not merely the difference of effects, is the true indicator of distinct natures.

Core Idea
Distinct natures are known by the absence of others' effects, not by differing effects alone.

Razi addresses a doubt about how we know the natures of water and fire are different. The common answer is that they produce different effects, but Razi refines this by pointing out that the real criterion is the possibility of one existing without the other's effect. This passage sharpens the kalam understanding of causality and essence.

Guided Path
1
Read the Passage
Read the Arabic passage and translation to grasp Razi's refutation of the fourth doubt.
Notice the key terms: طبيعة (nature), أثر (effect), تخلف الآثار (absence of effects).
2
Identify the Key Distinction
Contrast the two criteria: difference of effects (اختلاف الأثرين) versus absence of effects (تخلف الآثار). Understand why the latter is more fundamental.
3
Reflect on the Argument's Logic
Consider why the absence argument is stronger: it shows that water and fire are not necessarily tied to the other's effect, indicating distinct essences.
This connects to broader issues in causality and essence in kalam.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والجواب عن الشبهة الرابعة أن الذي به عرفنا أن طبيعة الماء تخالف طبيعة النار، ليس هو مجرد اختلاف الأثرين بل لما رأينا الماء حاصلا بدون التسخين، والنار حاصلة بدون التبريد علمنا بهذا اختلاف طبيعتيهما . فالحاصل : أن المعرف لاختلاف الطبيعتين هو تخلف الآثار. لا اختلاف الآثار.
And the answer to the fourth doubt: that that by which we know that the nature of water differs from the nature of fire is not merely the difference of the two effects, but because we see water occurring without heating, and fire occurring without cooling, we know by this the difference of their two natures. So the conclusion: that which identifies the difference of the two natures is the absence of effects, not the difference of effects.
Source pages p. 237

Commentary
This passage is a response to a doubt about how we know that the natures of water and fire are different. The doubt might be that we know they are different because they produce different effects (water cools, fire heats). Razi refines this: it is not merely the difference of effects, but the fact that each can exist without the effect of the other. Water can be present without heating, and fire can be present without cooling. Thus, the distinguishing criterion is not the presence of different effects but the possibility of one effect being absent while the other is present. In other words, the natures are different because the effects are mutually exclusive: one can exist without the other, which shows they are not necessarily linked. The last sentence summarizes: the identifier is the lack of the other's effect (takhalluf al-athar), not the mere difference.

Key Terms
طبيعة
أثر
تخلف الآثار

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Because water cools and fire heats.
B) Because water can exist without heating and fire without cooling.
C) Because they are both elements.
D) Because water is wet and fire is dry.

Key Takeaway
The true sign of distinct natures is not that they produce different effects, but that each can exist without the effect of the other.

Would your reliance on Allah increase? Identify one created thing you habitually fear or depend on, and pray for the strength to see its effect as a veil for the Creator's action.

Refuting Astrologers: The Argument from Simplicity and Composition
Razi argues that spheres must be either simple or composite, both leading to contingency.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Razi's rational argument that celestial bodies are contingent and require a creator.

Core Idea
All celestial bodies, whether simple or composite, are subject to change and thus need a Maker.

Razi confronts a third group of opponents: astrologers who claim the spheres and planets govern the lower world. He presents a powerful proof that these celestial bodies cannot be self-sufficient. Let's examine his reasoning step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage once without overthinking. Then read the translation. Let Razi's own phrasing guide you.
This is a dense philosophical argument; take your time.
2
Identify the dichotomy
Note the key division: spheres are either simple (بسيط) or composite (مركب). This is the starting point of the proof.
Simple means not composed of parts; composite means made of parts.
3
Understand the implication for simple bodies
If simple, they have two symmetrical sides. This allows rotation, which implies composition and decomposition are possible.
Razi uses the example of right and left sides being touched.
4
Follow the conclusion
Since composition and decomposition are possible, celestial bodies are not eternal or incorruptible. They depend on a free-acting agent.
This refutes both astrologers and philosophers like Ptolemy.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفرقة الثالثة من المخالفين في هذه المسألة: المنجمون الذين يقولون : المدبر في هذا العالم السفلي هو الأفلاك والكواكب والذي يدل على بطلان قولهم : إن الأفلاك إما أن تكون بسائط أو مركبة من أجزاء كل واحد منها بسيط في نفسه . بسيط فإنه يحصل له جانبان بحيث يكون كل واحد منهما مساويا للآخر - بكل ما كان كذلك . - فما كان ممسوس يمينه صح أن ينقلب ممسوس يساره، وبالعكس. كل ما كان كذلك، فإن التركيب والانحلال جائزان عليه . فإذن التركيب والانحلال جائزان على أجرام الفلك والكواكب. فيلزم من هذه النكتة فساد أصول الفلاسفة، أصحاب المجسطي في أجرام الفلك. ويلزم القول بافتقارها في ذواتها وصفاتها وأشكالها إلى تقدير فاعل مختار. واحتجوا : بأنا نشاهد أن تغيرات أحوال هذا العالم مربوطة بتغيرات أحوال الكواكب ويدل عليه حال الليل والنهار وحال الفصول الأربعة. والجواب : ثبت في المنطق أنه لا يلزم من حصول شيء عند شيء، ومن عدمه عند عدمه، كونه معللا به لاحتمال حصول هذا الدوران مع شرط العلة وشرط العلة صفة لازمة للعلة طردا وعكسا ، أنه يكون أجنبيا عن التأثير.
The third group of opponents in this issue: the astrologers (al-munajjimun) who say that the governor in this lower world is the spheres and planets. And what indicates the falsehood of their statement: that the spheres are either simple or composed of parts each of which is simple in itself. If simple, it acquires two sides such that each of them is equal to the other — and everything that is like that — then whatever has its right side touched can be turned into having its left side touched, and vice versa. Everything like that, composition and decomposition are possible upon it. So then composition and decomposition are possible upon the celestial bodies and planets. From this subtle point follows the corruption of the principles of the philosophers, the authors of the Almagest, regarding celestial bodies. And it follows the necessity of their dependence, in their essences, attributes, and forms, upon the determination of a free-acting agent. And they argued: we observe that the changes of the states of this world are linked to the changes of the states of the planets, and the case of night and day and the four seasons indicates that. The answer: it is established in logic that from the occurrence of something at something, and from its absence at its absence, it does not necessarily follow that it is caused by it, because of the possibility of this correlation occurring with a condition of the cause, and the condition of the cause is a necessary attribute of the cause both positively and negatively, [and] that it is extraneous to the effect.
Source pages p. 237

Commentary
This passage refutes the astrologers who claim that celestial bodies govern the lower world. Razi first presents a proof: consider whether the spheres are simple or composite. If simple, they have symmetrical sides, allowing for rotation and exchange of sides, which implies composition and decomposition are possible. If composite, that is obvious. Therefore, all celestial bodies are subject to composition and decomposition. This undermines the philosophers' (e.g., Ptolemy) view that celestial bodies are incorruptible. They must depend on a Maker. Then Razi addresses their objection: they observe correlations, like night/day and seasons, with planetary movements. The answer is logical: correlation does not imply causation. It could be that the correlation is merely conditional, not causal. The condition is a necessary concomitant of the cause, not the cause itself. This is a standard refutation against astrological determinism.

Key Terms
المنجمون
الأفلاك
الكواكب
بسائط
مركبة
فاعل مختار

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Celestial bodies are eternal and self-sufficient.
Correction: Razi argues they are subject to composition and decomposition, hence contingent and need a Creator.

Checkpoint
What does Razi conclude from the possibility of composition and decomposition in celestial bodies?
A) They are eternal.
B) They depend on a free-acting agent.
C) They are made of earth.
D) They are self-governing.

Key Takeaway
Razi's dilemma proves that all celestial bodies are contingent, undermining astrological and philosophical claims of their independence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you ever find yourself secretly relying on created things—like luck, natural laws, or even your own abilities—as if they were independent of Allah's control? How would Razi's argument challenge that tendency in your heart?
?
If celestial bodies, despite their grandeur, are contingent and need a Maker, what does that say about any created thing you might be tempted to fear or rely on more than Allah?
?
When you face uncertainty, do you turn to Allah as the only fāʿil mukhtār, or do you seek reassurance from worldly means? What would it take for you to truly internalize that nothing—not even the stars—has any power independent of Him?

Refuting Astrologers: Correlation Does Not Imply Causation
Razi refutes the astrological argument from correlation with a logical principle.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why observed correlations (e.g., seasons and planetary movements) do not prove causation.

Core Idea
Even if events coincide, logical causality is not proven—it may be mere correlation.

Astrologers object: we see clear links—night/day, seasons—with planetary movements. Razi responds with a logical principle from Aristotelian logic: correlation does not establish causation. Let's see how he dismantles their objection.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and response
Read the Arabic and translation. Focus on the astrologers' claim and Razi's answer.
The objection is based on observation; the answer is based on logic.
2
Identify the logical principle
Razi states that mere conjunction (occurrence together) does not prove causation. It could be that both are effects of a common cause or that one is a condition.
This is a foundational principle in logic and kalam.
3
Apply to the examples
Consider night/day and seasons: these correlate with planetary movements, but might be due to a shared cause (e.g., divine decree) rather than direct influence.
The astrologers' inference is logically invalid.

Refuting the Naturalists: The Impossibility of Mixture as a Cause
Razi refutes naturalists who claim that all events are due to mixture of elements.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Razi's logical refutation of the naturalist doctrine that mixture (al-mizaj) of elements causes the events of the world.

Core Idea
Mixture cannot cause events—it leads to logical contradictions.

Imam Razi now addresses the naturalists (al-tabi'iyyun), who claim that the world's events—minerals, plants, and animals—are caused solely by the mixing of the four elements. In this passage, he presents a devastating logical dilemma: either the elements' mutual influence happens simultaneously or sequentially, both of which lead to contradictions. He then responds to a potential objection, showing that the argument stands. Let's work through his reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice the structure: claim, dilemma, two horns, objection, answer.
The passage is dense; take your time.
2
Identify the naturalist claim and Razi's strategy
The naturalists say mixture causes all events. Razi presents a dilemma: either simultaneous or sequential interaction. Understand each horn.
3
Analyze the two horns of the dilemma
First horn: simultaneous influence leads to contradiction because causes would be at full strength yet broken. Second horn: sequential influence impossible because the overcome cannot later become overcomer.
This is a classical reductio ad absurdum.
4
Understand the objection and Razi's rebuttal
The objection distinguishes between qualities and their effects. Razi rebuts that opposition is through effects, so the dilemma remains.
The rebuttal reaffirms the original argument.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفرقة الرابعة من المخالفين في هذه المسألة الطبيعيون. وهم يقولون : إن حوادث هذا العالم من المعادن والنبات والحيوان معللة بامتزاج هذه العناصر بعضها مع بعض. واعلم أن القول بالمزاج باطل. وذلك لأن هذه العناصر إذا اختلطت وامتزجت فإما أن يكون تأثير كل واحد منها في الآخر، وتأثر كل واحد منها عن الآخر، يقع دفعة وإما أن يوجد تأثير أحدهما في الآخر، ثم بعد ذلك يعود ذلك المغلوب غالبا. والأول باطل. لأن المؤثر في انكسار كل واحد منهما : قوة الآخر. فالحار والبارد لو حصل انكسارهما معا - وحال حصول المعلول لابد من حصول العلة - يلزم أن يكونا في غاية القوة حال كونهما منكسرين وذلك محال. والثاني باطل أيضا لأن المغلوب بعد صيرورته مغلوبا، يمتنع أن يعود غالبا مع أنه حال قوته كان عاجزا عن قهره. لا يقال : لم لا يجوز أن يقال النارية صفة قائمة بجسم النار، وتلك الصفة علة للحرارة واليبوسة وكذا المائية صفة قائمة بالماء، وتلك الصفة علة للبرودة والرطوبة. فالكاسر لبرد الماء ورطوبته ليس هو حر النار ويبسها بل ناريتها والكاسر لحر النار ويبسها ليس هو برد الماء ورطوبته، بل مائيته ؟ وعلى هذا التقدير يزول السؤال لأنا نقول : النارية والمائية إنما يتنافيان بواسطة ما بين أثريهما من التنافي من التنافي وحينئذ يعود الكلام الأول
The fourth group of opponents in this issue: the naturalists (al-tabi'iyyun). They say that the events of this world — minerals, plants, and animals — are caused by the mixture (imtizaj) of these elements with each other. Know that the claim of mixture is false. Because when these elements mix and blend, either the influence of each upon the other and the being influenced of each by the other occurs simultaneously, or the influence of one upon the other occurs, then after that the overcome becomes the overcomer. The first is false because the factor in the breaking of each is the force of the other. So if heat and cold both became broken simultaneously — and in the state of the effect's occurrence the cause must exist — it would necessitate that they be in the utmost strength at the moment they are broken, which is impossible. The second is also false because the overcome, after becoming overcome, cannot become overcomer again, especially since at the time of its strength it was unable to overcome it. It cannot be said: why is it not permissible to say that fieriness (al-nariyya) is a quality subsisting in the body of fire, and that quality is the cause of heat and dryness, and likewise wateriness (al-ma'iyya) is a quality subsisting in water, and that quality is the cause of cold and wetness. So the breaker of the cold and wetness of water is not the heat and dryness of fire but its fieriness, and the breaker of the heat and dryness of fire is not the cold and wetness of water but its wateriness? On this assumption the question disappears. For we say: fieriness and wateriness are mutually opposed only by means of the opposition between their effects, and then the first argument returns.
Source pages p. 238

Commentary
This passage refutes the naturalists who attribute all natural events (minerals, plants, animals) to the mixing of elements. Razi argues that mixture (al-mizaj) is false. He presents a dilemma: when elements mix, either their mutual influence happens simultaneously or sequentially. If simultaneous, the effect of breaking each other would require the causes (the opposing forces) to be at their peak at the moment they break each other, which is a contradiction. If sequential, the overcome element cannot later become the overcomer because it was already weak. Then there is an objection: one might argue that it's not the heat and cold themselves that break each other, but the inherent qualities 'fieriness' and 'wateriness.' The answer is that these qualities oppose each other only through their effects, so the original argument about simultaneous breaking still applies. Thus, mixture cannot be the cause.

Key Terms
الطبيعيون
المزاج
العناصر
النارية
المائية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The mixture of elements directly causes the generation of minerals, plants, and animals.
Correction: Razi shows that mixture leads to logical contradictions, so it cannot be the true cause.

Checkpoint
What dilemma does Razi present against the naturalist claim that mixture of elements causes events?
A) The elements are eternal.
B) The mutual influence of elements must be either simultaneous or sequential, and both lead to contradictions.
C) The elements cannot mix.
D) The naturalists have no proof.

Key Takeaway
Razi's dilemma demonstrates that the naturalist theory of mixture is logically untenable, as it leads to contradictions regardless of how the interaction between elements is conceived.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you ever find yourself attributing the events of your life solely to material causes, as if nature runs on autopilot? How does Razi's logical refutation challenge that hidden assumption in your heart? Today, consciously pause before one event and say: 'This is from Allah, not from the mixture of elements.'
?
Razi shows that a contradiction arises when we assume opposing forces can simultaneously break each other. In your own life, are you holding onto contradictory beliefs—like trusting in secondary causes while professing tawheed? Identify one such contradiction and resolve it by affirming Allah's sole causality.
?
The naturalists' error begins with a seemingly plausible idea (mixture explains generation). What plausible but false beliefs have you accepted without testing their logical consistency? Commit to examining one such belief this week with the same rigor Razi applies here.

Refutation of the Naturalists' Theory of Mixture
Razi refutes the naturalist claim that mixture causes events.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Razi's logical refutation of the claim that events are caused by mixture of elements.

Core Idea
Mixture cannot cause events because simultaneous or sequential interaction is logically impossible.

The naturalists argued that the variety of the world arises from mixing elements. Razi challenges this by exposing a dilemma: either elements interact at once or in sequence. Both lead to contradiction. This passage walks through his argument.

Guided Path
1
Identify the naturalist claim
Read the first sentence where Razi states the naturalist thesis: events come from mixture of elements.
This sets up the target of refutation.
2
Follow the dilemma construction
Notice Razi presents two possibilities: simultaneous interaction or sequential interaction. Understand why these are the only options.
A dilemma forces the opponent into a corner.
3
Examine the first horn: simultaneous
Razi argues that if elements affect each other at once, each must be both at full strength and broken, which is contradictory.
This is a reductio ad absurdum.
4
Examine the second horn: sequential
If one overpowers then the vanquished returns, the vanquished must regain strength despite failing when strong – also impossible.
The logic appeals to clear physical impossibility.
5
Analyze the objection and response
An objector suggests the breaking is due to essences, not qualities. Razi counters that essences oppose through their effects, so the same dilemma applies.
This shows the argument is robust against this dodge.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفرقة الرابعة من المخالفين في هذه المسألة الطبيعيون. وهم يقولون : إن حوادث هذا العالم من المعادن والنبات والحيوان معللة بامتزاج هذه العناصر بعضها مع بعض.
واعلم أن القول بالمزاج باطل. وذلك لأن هذه العناصر إذا اختلطت وامتزجت فإما أن يكون تأثير كل واحد منها في الآخر، وتأثر كل واحد منها عن الآخر، يقع دفعة وإما أن يوجد تأثير أحدهما في الآخر، ثم بعد ذلك يعود ذلك المغلوب غالبا. والأول باطل. لأن المؤثر في انكسار كل واحد منهما : قوة الآخر. فالحار والبارد لو حصل انكسارهما معا - وحال حصول المعلول لابد من حصول العلة - يلزم أن يكونا في غاية القوة حال كونهما منكسرين وذلك محال. والثاني باطل أيضا لأن المغلوب بعد صيرورته مغلوبا، يمتنع أن يعود غالبا مع أنه حال قوته كان عاجزا عن قهره،
لا يقال : لم لا يجوز أن يقال النارية صفة قائمة النار، وتلك الصفة علة للحرارة واليبوسة وكذا المائية صفة قائمة بالماء، وتلك الصفة علة للبرودة والرطوبة. فالكاسر لبرد الماء ورطوبته ليس هو حر النار ويبسها بل ناريتها والكاسر لحر النار ويبسها ليس هو برد الماء ورطوبته، بل مائيته ؟
وعلى هذا التقدير يزول السؤال لأنا نقول : النارية والمائية إنما يتنافيان بواسطة ما بين أثريهما من التنافي وحينئذ يعود الكلام الأول
The fourth group of dissenters on this issue: the naturalists. They say: The events of this world, from minerals, plants, and animals, are caused by the mixture of these elements with one another.
Know that the statement about mixture is false. This is because if these elements mix and blend, then either the effect of each on the other and the being-affected of each by the other occurs at once, or the effect of one on the other occurs, then after that the vanquished one returns to being victorious. The first is false because what causes the breaking of each is the force of the other. So if heat and cold are both broken simultaneously—and at the occurrence of the effect, the cause must be present—it would necessitate that they are at the utmost strength while being broken, and that is impossible. The second is also false because the vanquished, after having become vanquished, cannot return to being victorious, given that when it was strong it was incapable of overcoming it.
It cannot be said: Why is it not permissible to say that fieriness (al-nāriyya) is an attribute subsisting in fire, and that attribute is the cause of heat and dryness? Likewise, wateriness (al-mā'iyya) is an attribute subsisting in water, and that attribute is the cause of cold and wetness. So the breaker of the cold and wetness of water is not the heat and dryness of fire but its fieriness, and the breaker of the heat and dryness of fire is not the cold and wetness of water but its wateriness? On this estimation, the question would be removed. But we say: Fieriness and wateriness are in opposition by virtue of the opposition between their effects, and then the original argument returns.
Source pages p. 238

Commentary
This passage presents Razi's refutation of the naturalist theory that the events of this world (minerals, plants, animals) are caused by the mixture (imtizāj) of the four elements (fire, air, water, earth). Razi first states the naturalist claim, then argues it is false. He presents a dilemma: when elements mix, either the interaction of each occurs simultaneously, or one overpowers the other and then returns to dominance. He refutes both possibilities. If simultaneous, each element must be both powerful and broken simultaneously, which is impossible because the cause of breaking is the other's force. If sequential, the vanquished element cannot return to victory because it was unable to overcome when it was strong. An objection is raised: perhaps the breaking is due to the essence (fieriness and wateriness) not the physical qualities. Razi responds that the essences themselves are in opposition through their effects, so the same problem recurs. Thus the naturalist claim is refuted.

العناصر
النارية
المائية
الانكسار
التنافي
العلة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Mixture of elements directly produces the variety of the world without divine causation.
Correction: Razi shows that the claimed causal interaction leads to logical contradictions, thus the theory cannot explain events.

Checkpoint
According to Razi, what logical impossibility arises if elements affect each other simultaneously?
A) The cause would be absent
B) The elements would be at full strength while being broken
C) The vanquished cannot return
D) The effects would be contradictory

Key Takeaway
Razi refutes naturalist causality by showing that mixture leads to logical contradictions, proving that events cannot be explained by elemental mixture alone.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you see a plant grow from soil, do you truly attribute its growth to the mixture of elements, or do you recognize the direct power of Allah? How would your reliance on natural causes change if you internalized Razi's logical refutation?
?
Razi shows that logical contradictions arise when we attribute causation to mixture. In your daily life, are there events you assume are caused by natural processes without considering the immediate act of Allah? What would it mean to live with the constant awareness that Allah is the direct creator of every effect?
?
Reflect on a time you felt frustrated because a natural cause failed—like medicine not working. Did you subconsciously assume that the mixture of ingredients must bring about health? How should your response to such situations be transformed by this lesson?

Causality and Created Things
advanced
8 min
Ash'ari Refutation of Tawallud
Ash'ari logical proof against generated acts (tawallud).

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Ash'ari argument that the theory of generated acts (tawallud) leads to the logical impossibility of one effect having two independent causes.

Core Idea
One effect cannot have two truly independent causes.

The Mu'tazilites believed that human actions could 'generate' effects in separate bodies, like a push causing movement in another object. The Ash'aris argued this leads to a logical contradiction. In this passage, Imam al-Razi presents a crisp proof using the example of two men acting on a single atom, showing that tawallud would require either two simultaneous movements in one atom or one movement caused by two independent agents—both impossible. Prepare to follow a tight, step-by-step argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the English translation. Let the logical flow of the argument sink in before we unpack it.
The translation is literal; some terms may feel unfamiliar.
2
Identify the core claim
Find where the Ash'ari state the main problem with tawallud: 'it would necessitate the occurrence of one effect from two independent causes.' This is the heart of the argument.
The phrase 'أثر واحد بمؤثرين مستقلين' is key.
3
Follow the example
Work through the example of two men, one pulling and one pushing, on a hand holding a single atom. Notice the two possibilities discussed: either two movements (absurd) or one movement from two causes (also absurd).
4
Understand the conclusion
The Ash'ari conclude that since both alternatives are impossible, the premise (tawallud) must be false. Reflect on why this refutation is considered decisive in Ash'ari thought.
The argument does not rely on empirical observation but on logical necessity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأيضا: الرجل إذا اعتمد على جسم آخر، فحدثت حركة في ذلك الجسم الآخر، فقد اتفقت المعتزلة على أن الحركة الحاصلة في ذلك الجسم المباين، إنما حصلت بتأثير وهذه هي المسألة المشهورة بمسألة التولد بالاعتماد وتأثير هذه المدافعة. وأصحابنا أنكروا ذلك. وحجة أصحابنا: أنه لو صح القول بالتولد، لزم وقوع الأثر الواحد بمؤثرين مستقلين بالأثر. وهذا محال فالقول بالتولد محال بيان الملازمة: أنه إذا التصق جوهر فرد بكف رجلين ثم إن أحدهم جذب الكف في حال ما دفع الآخر أيضا كفه. فلو صح القول بالتولد، كان الجذب مولدا للحركة في ذلك الجوهر، كما أن الدفع مولد للحركة واحدة والأول باطل، لأنه يقتضي حصول الجسم الواحد، في الحيز، في الآن الواحد مرتين. وهذا غير معقول. وأيضا: فعلى هذا التقدير تكون الحركتان متماثلتين، فليس استناد أحدهما، إلى الجذب والثانية إلى الدفع أولى من العكس فيلزم استناد كل واحدة من الحركتين إلى الجذب وإلى الدفع، فيعود الأمر إلى وقوع الأثر الواحد بمؤثرين مستقلين. ولما بطل هذا القسم ثبت أنه حصل في ذلك الجوهر الفرد حركة واحدة، حصلت بعلة الجذب وبعلة الدفع ثم كل واحدة من هاتين العلتين مستقلة باقتضاء هذا الأثر مع القول بالتولد فيلزم حصول الأثر الواحد بمؤثرين مستقلين وذلك محال. لأن ذلك الأثر يستغني بكل واحد منهما عن كل واحد منهما. وهو محال.
And also: [If] a man leans on another body, and a movement occurs in that other body, the Mu'tazilites have agreed that the movement occurring in that separate body only occurs through an effect, and this is the well-known issue of generation [tawallud] through leaning and the effect of this mutual repulsion. Our companions [the Ash'aris] denied that. The proof of our companions: If the statement about generation were valid, it would necessitate the occurrence of one effect from two independent causes. This is impossible, so the statement about generation is impossible. The explanation of the entailment: When a single atom adheres to the palm of two men, then one of them pulls the palm while the other also pushes his palm. If the statement about generation were valid, the pulling would generate the movement in that atom, just as the pushing generates [movement]—and the first [possibility] is invalid because it would require the same body to occupy a single location twice in one moment. This is unintelligible. And also: On this assumption the two movements would be similar, and there would be no reason to ascribe one to the pulling and the other to the pushing rather than the opposite, so it would follow that each of the two movements is ascribed to both pulling and pushing, and the matter returns to the occurrence of one effect from two independent causes. And when this division is invalidated, it is established that in that single atom there occurred one movement, which occurred by the cause of pulling and by the cause of pushing, and then each of these two causes is independent in necessitating this effect along with the statement about generation, so the occurrence of one effect from two independent causes follows, and that is impossible. Because that effect would be independent of each of them due to each of them—and this is impossible.
Source pages p. 239

Commentary
This passage is an Ash'ari proof against the Mu'tazilite theory of tawallud (generated actions). The Ash'aris argue that tawallud leads to one effect having two independent causes, which is impossible. The example: two men pulling and pushing a hand that holds an atom. If tawallud is true, both pulling and pushing would each independently generate a movement in the atom, leading to either two movements in one atom at one time (impossible) or one movement caused by both—which means one effect from two independent causes, also impossible. Therefore tawallud is false. The proof refutes the Mu'tazilite notion that human actions can generate effects in separate bodies.

Key Terms
تولد
أثر واحد
مؤثرين مستقلين
جوهر فرد
جذب
دفع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think tawallud is like natural causality (e.g., fire burning) where one cause produces one effect, but tawallud allows multiple agents to generate the same effect independently.
Correction: The Ash'ari argument shows that if two agents each independently generate the same effect, it leads to absurdity; therefore, tawallud cannot be a valid account of causality.

Checkpoint
What is the main contradiction the Ash'ari derive from assuming tawallud?
A) It would require two movements in one atom at one time.
B) It would require the same effect to be caused by two independent causes.
C) It would require the atom to be in two places simultaneously.
D) It would require the puller and pusher to be the same person.

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazilite theory of generated acts is refuted by showing it implies the absurdity of a single effect having two truly independent causes.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you witness events in your daily life, do you ever feel that created causes are fully independent in producing their effects? How does this lesson challenge that feeling, and what can you do to remind yourself that Allah is the only true agent?
?
The Ash'ari argument shows the absurdity of attributing independent causality to creatures alongside Allah. In what areas of your life do you rely on created means as if they were independent of Allah's will? Take a moment to identify one such area and commit to mentally affirming Allah's sole agency in it.
?
This lesson refutes the notion that a human action can truly 'generate' an effect outside itself. Have you ever credited your own effort or skill for a success without thanking Allah? How would acknowledging Allah's exclusive creative power change your attitude toward your achievements?

Causality and Created Things

Refutation of the Doctrine of Generated Acts (Tawallud)
Al-Razi refutes the Mu'tazilite doctrine of generated acts (tawallud).

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the Ash'arite refutation of the doctrine of tawallud (generated acts) is based on the impossibility of a single effect arising from two independent causes.

Core Idea
No created act can independently generate another act without God's direct involvement.

Imam al-Razi now takes up the Mu'tazilite doctrine of tawallud—the idea that created beings can produce effects independently. He argues that if this were true, it would lead to the absurdity of a single effect being produced by two independent causes, which is logically impossible. This lesson walks through his proof using the example of two men pulling and pushing a single atom.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the translation. Notice the logical structure: statement of denial, proof, example, consequences, conclusion.
2
Analyze the example of the atom and two palms
Al-Razi presents a concrete scenario: a single atom attached to two men's palms, one pulling, one pushing. Under tawallud, both actions would generate motion. Ask yourself: what would this entail?
3
Identify the two absurd consequences
First, it would require one body to occupy the same place twice in one instant. Second, since motions are identical, each motion would be attributed to both causes, leading to one effect from two independent agents.
4
Understand the final argument
Even if only one motion results, it is caused by both pulling and pushing, and each cause is independent in producing that effect under tawallud, again leading to one effect from two independent agents.
5
Note the Mu'tazila's argument and its refutation
Al-Razi mentions that the Mu'tazila rely on tawallud to justify praise and blame, but he says the answer has already been given in the issue of creation of acts. This ties back to earlier discussions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المسألة الثالثة والعشرون
في بيان إبطال القول بالتولد. وأصحابنا أنكروا ذلك.
وحجة أصحابنا : أنه لو صح القول بالتولد، لزم وقوع الأثر الواحد بمؤثرين مستقلين بالأثر . وهذا محال فالقول بالتولد محال بيان الملازمة: أنه إذا التصق جوهر فرد بكف رجلين ثم إن أحدهم جذب الكف في حال ما دفع الآخر أيضا كفه . فلو صح القول بالتولد، كان الجذب مولدا للحركة في ذلك الجوهر، كما أن الدفع مولد للحركة واحدة والأول باطل، لأنه يقتضي حصول الجسم الواحد، في الحيز، في الآن الواحد مرتين. وهذا غير معقول.
وأيضا : فعلى هذا التقدير تكون الحركتان متماثلتين، فليس استناد أحدهما إلى الجذب والثانية إلى الدفع أولى من العكس فيلزم استناد كل واحدة من الحركتين إلى الجذب وإلى الدفع، فيعود الأمر إلى وقوع الأثر الواحد بمؤثرين مستقلين. ولما بطل هذا القسم ثبت أنه حصل في ذلك الجوهر الفرد حركة واحدة، حصلت بعلة الجذب وبعلة الدفع ثم كل واحدة من هاتين العلتين مستقلة باقتضاء هذا الأثر مع القول بالتولد فيلزم حصول الأثر الواحد بمؤثرين مستقلين وذلك محال . لأن ذلك الأثر يستغني بكل واحد منهما عن كل واحد منهما. وهو محال. واستدلال المعتزلة على القول بالتولد : إنما هو لحسن المدح والذم والثواب والعقاب. وقد تقدم الجواب عنه في مسألة خلق الأفعال.
The twenty-third issue: On explaining the refutation of the doctrine of generation (tawallud). Our companions denied that. The proof of our companions: if the doctrine of generation were valid, it would entail the occurrence of a single effect by two independent agents. That is impossible, so the doctrine of generation is impossible. Explanation of the implication: when a single atom attaches to the palm of two men, and then one of them pulls the palm while the other also pushes his palm, then if the doctrine of generation were valid, the pulling would generate motion in that atom just as the pushing generates motion. [But] one motion [would result] from two causes, and the first [case] is invalid because it requires one body to occupy one place twice in one moment. That is inconceivable. Also, on this assumption, the two motions would be identical, so the attribution of one to pulling and the other to pushing is not more appropriate than the opposite, thus it would be necessary to attribute each of the two motions to both pulling and pushing, reverting to the occurrence of a single effect by two independent agents. Since this case is invalid, it is established that a single motion occurs in that atom, caused by pulling and by pushing, and then each of these two causes is independent in necessitating this effect under the doctrine of generation, so it entails the occurrence of a single effect by two independent agents, and that is impossible because that effect would be independent of each of them by means of each of them. That is impossible. The Mu'tazila's argument for the doctrine of generation is only based on the validity of praise, blame, reward, and punishment. The answer to it has already been given in the issue of the creation of acts.
Source pages p. 240

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the Mu'tazilite doctrine of tawallud (generated acts), which holds that created beings can produce effects independently of God. Al-Razi begins by stating that the Ash'arites deny this doctrine. He then provides a proof: if tawallud were true, it would allow a single effect to be produced by two independent causes, which is impossible. To show the implication, he gives an example: if a single atom is attached to the palms of two men, and one pulls while the other pushes, under tawallud both actions would generate motion in that atom. This leads to two absurd consequences. First, it would require the same body to occupy the same place twice in one instant, which is inconceivable. Second, assuming two motions are identical, there is no reason to attribute one motion to pulling and the other to pushing rather than the opposite, so each motion would have to be attributed to both causes, again yielding a single effect from two independent agents. Since both scenarios fail, only one motion occurs, caused by both pulling and pushing, but each cause would still be independent in producing that effect under tawallud, again leading to the impossible scenario of one effect from two independent agents. Al-Razi concludes by noting that the Mu'tazila's reliance on tawallud to justify praise, blame, reward, and punishment has already been answered in the discussion on the creation of acts.

Key Terms
تولد
جوهر فرد
مؤثرين مستقلين

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Mu'tazilite doctrine of tawallud holds that created beings can generate acts independently of God, which is used to justify human accountability for actions.
Correction: Al-Razi argues that tawallud leads to logical absurdities, and that true causality belongs only to God. The Ash'arite position is that all acts are created by God directly, while human acquisition (kasb) is the basis for moral responsibility.

Checkpoint
What is the main consequence al-Razi uses to refute the doctrine of tawallud?
A) It leads to infinite regress.
B) It entails a single effect from two independent causes.
C) It denies human free will.
D) It contradicts the Quran.

Key Takeaway
The doctrine of tawallud is impossible because it entails the logical contradiction of a single effect being produced by two independent causes.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of a moment when you felt proud of an achievement, attributing it solely to your own effort. How does Al-Razi's demonstration that no created act can independently generate another act challenge that feeling? What would it look like—right now—to genuinely attribute every outcome directly to God's power?
?
Al-Razi's argument shows that the idea of generated acts leads to logical contradictions. But doesn't your daily experience feel as though your actions produce real effects? Are you willing to say that this feeling is an illusion, and that God alone creates every movement, every thought, every breath? What would change if you actually lived that belief today?
?
The Mu'tazilites insisted on tawallud to preserve moral accountability. If God creates all acts, including your sins, how do you reconcile that with your sense of guilt and responsibility? Do you dismiss it as a theological puzzle, or does it press you to examine what it means to 'own' your actions before God? Ask yourself: am I ready to stand before Him and say, 'You created this sin in me, so I am not responsible'? Or do you feel the weight of accountability still? Let this unease drive you to seek deeper understanding.

Causality and Created Things
